## ART PLACEHOLDER 

150 

## Overview 

_lades ’68_ is a supplement for _Blades in the Dark_ and uses almost all the same core mechanics. You can swap in any modules from _Blades in the_ B _Dark: Deep Cuts._ You could easily replace the action roll with the threat roll, for example. 

There are many changes to the **gameplay cycle** , score aftermath, entanglements, and downtime—these systems are covered in full in the **Gameplay Cycle** chapters (page 162). There are also a few core mechanics changed to reflect the different tone of _Blades ’68_ . 

- **Harm** (page 152). Harm is just another consideration that factors into effect, and carries no other mechanical penalties. Instead, PCs roll for **harm complications** (page 192) after the score to see how they are impacted. 

- **Resistance** (page 153). You can resist any stepped consequence, such as harm or clock ticks, multiple times (reducing the consequence once each time). **Armor** reduces all incoming harm, but takes 3 load. 

- **Keys and deadlocks** (page 154). **Keys** are character traits. You can get 1 xp per session if your character does something that expresses one of their keys (like someone with the “charming” key trying to be charming). Pick two or three to start with. **Deadlocks** replace trauma. Like trauma, you take a deadlock when you run out of Stress, and you can gain xp from them, and you have to retire when you have four deadlocks. 

- **Stacks and bank** (page 158). Instead of coin and stash, characters track **stacks** of cash, and can put stacks in the **bank** . 

- **Advancement** (page 160). Desperate actions earn xp as usual, but so does rolling a **zero-dot action** —any action you have no rating in (if you then gain some dice from pushing or help, you still mark xp). An action that’s both desperate and zero-dot earns 2 xp. Mark 1 xp when you **hit a key** , once per session. There are also two end-of-session triggers on your character sheet. In all these cases, including desperate actions or zero-dot actions, you can mark the xp on any track (an attribute like Prowess, or on your playbook xp track). 

151 

## **Harm** 

## **Harm is just another fictional consideration that flows into effect, and carries no other mechanical penalties during a score.** 

The GM should weigh up a character’s harm and consider whether they might have less effect than normal for any given action. Also consider the tone of your game—these characters are daring scoundrels engaged in hectic criminal capers, and may not always be slowed down by regular injuries. 

_Hadwig is trying to run away from the Bluecoats, but she has the critical harm “Gunshot Wound in Leg.” The GM decides it means limited effect._ 

_Pendleton is trying to convince the bouncer to let the crew into the nightclub. They have two level 1 harms (“Stressed” and “Dazed”), but everybody at the table has forgotten about the harm and it doesn’t really matter anyway, so it doesn’t change their effect._ 

If you take a mortal injury (level 4), your character will die very quickly without immediate and miraculous intervention. Write the nature of the lethal injury on your character sheet. If they happen to survive, it will surely leave a gnarly scar. If they die, they might come back as resonance echo, and their death wound will be part of their look. 

## **Harm Complications** 

The main way that harm hampers a character is through potential harm complications after the score. 

Immediately after the score, as part of the aftermath, every character with harm rolls for harm complications. 

See **Aftermath** , page 192, for harm complications. 

152 

## **Resistance** 

## **You can resist any stepped consequence, such as harm or clock ticks,** 

**multiple times** (reducing the consequence once each time). You can decide after each resistance roll, but make it quick. 

Resistance rolls will often resist the entire consequence in one go, at the GM’s discretion; they don’t always require multiple rolls. 

## **Armor** 

Armor is rare but not unknown on the streets of Doskvol ’68. Riotcoats, for example, wear an outfit that includes layers of impact-resistant fabric combined with areas of rigid plate, along with a full-face helmet. 

Armor costs 3 load. Hulls have built-in armor, which takes zero load. 

As long as you’re wearing armor, **all physical harm is reduced by one level** (think of it as an automatic resistance roll), every time. 

**Totaled:** You can choose to total your armor, which completely resists one instance of physical harm, but the armor is trashed in the process and won’t be any benefit for the rest of the score. 

**If you take level 3 physical harm** (after the effect of the armor and any resistance rolls are applied), **the armor is totaled automatically** (which also resists the harm in the process). 

The GM has final say whether armor is applicable to any given form of harm (e.g. poison gas). 

Like other items, armor is restored during personal business. 

## **Stab-Proof Vests and Other Light Armor** 

Light armor takes less load (2 slots) and is effective against just one instance of physical harm. It eliminates that harm consequence entirely, and is then totaled. Such armor can only be attained through an Acquire Asset downtime activity, or an invention project. 

See page 174 for this and other example assets. 

153 

## Keys and Deadlocks 

## **Keys and Deadlocks in Brief** 

See the next page for a full explanation of keys and deadlocks. If you’re playing with people already familiar with _Blades in the Dark_ , you could simply say: 

- Keys are character traits. You can get 1 xp per session if your character does something that expresses one of their keys (like someone with the “charming” key trying to be charming). Pick two or three to start with. 

- Deadlocks replace trauma. Like trauma, you take a deadlock when you run out of Stress, and you can gain xp from them, and you have to retire when you have four deadlocks. 

Leave the rest of the details (marking and turning keys) until they come up in play. 

## **Keys** 

Keys are the personality traits of your character. They help you decide what kind of person your PC is before you start. You get xp for portraying your keys in play. 

Keys include personality traits like _protective, confident, curious, kind, reckless, enthusiastic, brooding, shy._ 

You can have up to four keys, but you don’t need all four to start. Most people discover who their character is through play, and it’s good to leave space to do so. 

There are example keys at the bottom of each playbook sheet; pick from these, or use ones from another playbook, or make up your own. 

Keys are not a moral judgment. They are not “good” or “bad,” they’re just who your character is. Change your keys at any time if you think a different one would fit better. 

## **Hitting a Key** 

**Once per session** when you showcase one of your keys through play, tell the rest of the table, mark the key on your character sheet, **and** mark 1 xp on any track. 

## _Charming_ 

**That’s a maximum of one key, once per session, for a total of 1 xp from your keys per session.** 

154 

You can and should roleplay all your keys all the time, but it’s still just 1 xp per session. 

When a key has been marked three times, you can continue to get xp for it, but you don’t need to keep marking it. 

Tell the table when you’ve hit a key, and mark it immediately. If you forget to do this during the session, it’s fine to do it retroactively when you’re going over end-of-session xp. Tell everyone what it was, and mark the key and xp as usual. 

## **Deadlocks** 

When you run out of Stress (marking the last Stress box), your character has reached a moment of crisis. They’re out of the action (for a few moments, or maybe the rest of the scene), but once they’ve gathered themself, they come back with zero Stress. 

When this happens, mark the explosion box next to a key. 

## _Charming_ 

That key is now deadlocked; cross it out. You can no longer mark it for xp, and it no longer applies to your character. Instead, write a corresponding **deadlock** . 

## _Charming_ 

## _Cold_ 

A deadlock should relate to its key in some way; perhaps the opposite trait, or perhaps an intensification. Either way, a character trait that was core to your character’s career as a scoundrel has been changed forever. 

Example deadlocks: 

- The key _**idealistic**_ could flip to the deadlock _cynical_ , or it might intensify into _zealous_ . 

- The key _**sophisticated**_ could flip to the deadlock _modest_ , or it might intensify into _pretentious_ . 

- The key _**commanding**_ could flip to the deadlock _deferential_ , or it might intensify into _controlling_ . 

Once all four keys are deadlocked, your character can’t continue in a life of crime and must retire. 

Deadlocks are not always bad traits. If you have the key of _distant_ , it might deadlock to _warm_ —that’s a positive change for your character. What’s important, though, is that deadlocks make it more difficult for your character to continue their life of crime. Perhaps your PC needed to be _distant_ to navigate the underworld, and becoming a _warm_ person makes this more difficult. 

155 

The process of keys becoming deadlocks forms an important part of your character’s story arc, so consider the eventual deadlocks when choosing keys. Think not just about who your character is to begin with, but who they might become. 

You may be able to convert deadlocks back into keys (either the original key, or a new one). Negotiate a long-term project with the GM to do so, depending on the nature of the deadlock and how your character goes about it. 

If a deadlock turns back into a key, clear all the marks next to it. 

## **Turning a Key** 

If you’ve hit a key three times (and marked the three boxes), you can choose to **turn the key** at any time. 

## _Cautious_ 

When you turn a key, you take decisive action that reflects a major change of heart. **Deadlock** the key immediately and clear all your Stress, and the action you take when you turn the key is automatically a **critical success** (with no need to roll). You must decide before you roll any dice, not after. 

## _Cautious_ 

## _Reckless_ 

Because keys have to be deadlocked anyway when you run out of Stress, it may be worth looking for a dramatic opportunity to turn a key instead of waiting for a resistance roll to go bad. 

_Pendleton, a Swinger, was trying to a charm Veretta Keel but rolled a 1-3. They managed to resist being thrown out the window, but they did get slammed against the wall and they’re currently sitting on the floor, stunned. Keri marks Pendleton’s last Stress box, and has to choose a key to deadlock. He decides to deadlock “charming,” turning it to “cold.” In a few moments, Pendleton will come back to their senses, albeit with a new, frosty demeanor._ 

_Hadwig, an Operative, was cautiously hiding on the window ledge outside, watching all this happen (Olivia had declared this as hitting her key, marking “cautious” a third time, and marking 1 xp on her playbook track)._ 

_As Pendleton crumples to the ground and Veretta Keel flicks out a switchblade, Hadwig decides to intervene. Olivia declares that she’s going to smash through the window and tackle Veretta to the ground, with a goal of putting her silenced shooter to Veretta’s head. She’s thinking Prowl_ 

156 

_for the action. The GM suggests that this will be desperate position, with limited effect (meaning, in this case, that the action will cover smashing the window and tackling Veretta, but a second action will be required to get the gun to her head). Hadwig is already at 7 Stress, so things are tense._ 

_Olivia declares that she’s going to turn Hadwig’s key “cautious.” This is a moment of crisis, and Hadwig has been pushed to uncharacteristic recklessness. The action is an automatic critical success, which means increased effect—Hadwig smashes through the window, tackles Veretta to the ground, and puts her shooter to Veretta’s head. No roll needed. Hadwig clears all her Stress, and Olivia crosses out “cautious” and replaces it with “reckless.”_ 

157 

## Stacks and Bank 

## **Stacks** 

A **stack** is an abstract amount of money; as the name implies, a stack of cash. A week’s wages, a decent wad of bills. 

We don’t track money below this amount. All PCs are assumed to have a pocketful of coins at any given time, maybe a few paper notes in their wallet. The crew as a whole has its own ledger of incomings and outgoings that we also do not track to the last coin. 

If a character wants to spend money to achieve a small goal (bribe a bouncer), use the PC’s lifestyle quality for a fortune roll. 

## **Monetary Values** 

- **1 stack:** A fat wad of bills. A week’s wages. 

- **2 stacks:** A fine firearm. A fashionable suit. 

- **4 stacks:** A month’s wages. A briefcase full of cash. 

- **6 stacks:** A luxury watch or exquisite diamond. A stolen autopod. 

- **8 stacks:** A small safe full of cash and valuables. A very rare luxury commodity (a first-edition book, an original vinyl pressing, a sought-after vintage from a rarefied vineyard). 

- **10 stacks:** A significant asset—a luxury autopod, the deed to a small property, a painting by a world-famous artist, a yacht. 

More than 4 stacks is an impractical amount to keep lying around. You must spend the excess or put it in the bank (see the next page). 

A crew can also store 4 stacks in their base, by default. If they upgrade to a vault, they can expand their stores to 8 and then 16 stacks. Any stacks beyond their limit must be spent as soon as possible (typically before the next score) or distributed among the crew members. 

A stack of cash takes up one item slot for your load when carried. 

## **Spending Stacks** 

- Spend 1 stack to get an additional **downtime activity** during **personal business** . 

- Spend 1 stack to increase the result level of a **downtime activity** roll. 

- Spend stacks to avoid certain crew entanglements. 

- Put stacks into your character’s bank to improve their lifestyle and circumstances when they retire. 

- Spend stacks when you advance your crew’s **Tier** . 

158 

## **Bank** 

When you mark your character’s final **deadlock** and they retire, the amount of cash they’ve managed to bank determines their fate. Your bank tracker is on your character sheet. 

Like stacks, bank is an abstraction—it represents some combination of money stashed in physical hiding places, stocks, legitimate financial institutions, and underworld banks. 

## **Retirement** 

- **Bank 0** – **10: Down and out.** A derelict flophouse or crashing on couches. 

- **Bank 11** – **20: Getting by.** A modest home or apartment. You might run a bar or small business. 

- **Bank 21** – **39: Comfortable.** A stylish home or trendy apartment, with luxuries like a nice car or fashionable clothes. You might operate a medium business. 

- **Bank 40: Fine living.** A mansion, with the ability to afford luxuries like servants or a pleasure yacht. You no longer need to work if you don’t want to. 

In addition, each full row of bank (10 stacks) indicates the **Quality level of the character’s lifestyle** , from zero (street life) to four (luxury). Primarily this flows into **effect** , especially in social situations. If you can take someone to a nice restaurant, if you’re wearing impressive clothes, all of this might factor into increased effect for social actions. 

You can also roll lifestyle as a **fortune roll** —for example, when bribing a guard, you might make a lifestyle fortune roll first to see how big the bribe is. The outcome of that roll informs the subsequent action roll (whether Sway, or anything else), like a setup action. 

Finally, when you roll to **Acquire Asset** during downtime, roll the crew’s Tier with bonus dice equal to your personal lifestyle quality (full rows of bank). The more you have in the bank, the easier it is to acquire assets. 

## **Withdrawing Stacks from the Bank** 

If you want to pull stacks out of the bank, you may do so, at a cost. **For every 2 stacks removed from the bank, you get 1 stack in cash.** 

159 

## Advancement 

## **PC Advancement** 

**During the game session** , mark xp: 

- **When you make a desperate action roll.** When you roll in a group action that’s desperate, you also mark xp. 

- **When you make an action roll with a zero-dot rating.** This means any action you have no rating in. It’s OK if you then gain some bonus dice from pushing or assistance—you still mark xp. When you roll a zero-dot action as part of a group action, you also mark xp. An action that’s both desperate and zero-dot earns 2 xp. 

- **Mark 1 xp when you hit any key** (showcasing it in play), **once per session** . Tell the rest of the table when you do. 

In all these cases, you can mark the xp on any track (an attribute like Prowess, or on your playbook xp track). 

**At the end of the session** , review the xp triggers on your character sheet. For each one, mark 1 xp if it happened at all, or mark 2 xp if it happened a lot during the session. The xp triggers are: 

- **Your playbook-specific xp trigger.** For example, the Veteran’s is “Address a challenge with violence or discipline.” To “address a challenge,” your character should attempt to overcome a tough obstacle or threat. It doesn’t matter if the action is successful or not, you get xp either way. 

- **You struggled with issues stemming from a deadlock.** Mark xp for this if one of your deadlocks caused you trouble. 

- If you forgot to hit a key during the session, feel free to retroactively explain how you showcased one now, and mark 1 xp. 

Mark end-of-session xp on any xp tracks you want (any attribute or your playbook xp track). 

When you fill an xp track, clear all the marks and take an advance. When you take an advance from your playbook track, you may choose an additional special ability. When you take an advance from an attribute, you may add an additional action dot to one of the actions under that attribute. 

## **Crew Advancement** 

Crew advancement works the same as usual. See _Blades in the Dark_ , page 49. 

**Looking for Rep, hold, and increasing Tier?** See _Blades in the Dark_ , page 44. 

160 

161
