## Gameplay Cycle 

_Blades ’68_ has its own gameplay cycle, different to core _Blades in the Dark_ . Play moves through four typical phases, then cycles back around to the start. 

**1. Personal Business (page 164).** Personal business is free play—the characters just living their lives. They talk to each other, they go places, they do things, they make rolls as needed. This is sprinkled with downtime activities, which give the PCs the opportunity to indulge their vice to remove Stress, recover harm, work on a long-term project, or achieve other specific goals. When all downtime activities are complete and the PCs have done as much as they want to with free play, the crew gathers to pick their next score. 

**2. Planning Meeting (page 180).** The table collaborates to decide on the next score. Everyone pitches an opportunity, and then the players pick one to do next (and maybe also pick a secondary score for a cohort to tackle). Once a score is chosen, the GM will add some specific details to the broad opportunity. 

**3. The Score (page 188).** The PCs might choose to gather some information, then when they’re ready, they choose an approach and make an engagement roll. The GM cuts to the score in progress. The PCs engage the target—they make rolls, overcome obstacles, call for flashbacks, and complete the operation (successfully or not). 

**4. Aftermath (page 190).** During this short phase, we tally the fallout from the score, including payout, tithes, Rep, Heat, faction status changes, and harm complications. Aftermath ends with an unwind scene, as the crew debriefs and blows off steam together. The unwind scene is a capstone to the whole cycle, and transitions us back to the start (personal business). 

Once per gameplay cycle, the GM will run the **Trouble Engine** between sessions. This involves checking in with faction clocks and rolling for new trouble (just like entanglements in core _Blades_ ). Typically you might do this between gameplay cycles (after the aftermath and before personal business), but it’s flexible—do it whenever suits you best. 

It’s personal preference how long you spend on each phase. Most tables give one session for the personal business phase and one session per score. This is extremely flexible—big scores can easily take multiple sessions. It’s helpful to finish personal business with the planning meeting, so the GM has time to think about the next score between sessions. Aftermath can be wedged in wherever it fits, either at the end of the score or before personal business. 

163 

## Personal Business 

Personal business is a mix of free play and downtime activities—weave them together. During the personal business phase, you can still go places, do things, make action rolls, gather information, talk with other characters, etc. Only activities that are on the downtime activities list are limited. 

Personal business is the same as **downtime** in _Blades in the Dark_ , and you can easily swap in any _Deep Cuts_ modules that you like (such as diceless downtime). 

## **Backing Factions: Return the Favor** 

If any PC has a backing faction, make a Return the Favor roll at the start of personal business. Use one die for each instance of help received since the last time you made this roll. 

## **Return the Favor Results** 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0174-07.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1-3 Nothing, but add  +1d  next time you make this roll.<br>4/5 They ask for a  minor favor .<br>They give you a  personal mission . If you already have one active,<br>6<br>add one tick to the failure clock.<br>They give you an  urgent personal mission —mark the failure<br>Critical clock twice immediately. If you already have one active, add one<br>tick to the failure clock.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


See page 236 for more on backing factions, minor favors, and personal missions. 

## **Downtime Activities** 

You get **two free downtime activities** , and can pay **1 stack** or **1 Rep** for each additional downtime activity: 

- Indulge Vice 

- Long-Term Project 

- Recover 

- Reduce Heat 

- Acquire Asset 

- Training Montage 

164 

For any downtime activity, take **+1d** to the roll if a friend, rival, or contact helps you. Be liberal with this; it’s an excuse to bring your contacts and other PCs into your scenes. Use your rivals, even—who doesn’t enjoy a frenemy? 

After a downtime activity roll, you may spend **stacks** after the roll to improve the result level. **Increase the result level by one for each stack spent.** So, a 1-3 result becomes a 4/5, a 4/5 result becomes a 6, and a 6 becomes a critical. 

## **Indulge Vice** 

Roll to find out how much Stress your character clears. 

Make an **attribute roll** using your character’s lowest attribute rating (if there’s a tie, that’s fine—simply use that rating). **Clear Stress equal to the highest die result.** 

If you clear more Stress levels than you had marked, you **overindulge** . If you do not or cannot indulge your vice during downtime, you take Stress equal to your number of **deadlocks** . 

When you overindulge, you make a bad call because of your vice—in acquiring it or while under its influence. To bring the effect of this bad decision into the game, select an overindulgence from the list: 

- **Attract Trouble.** The GM selects or rolls an additional **Crew Trouble** (or Local Trouble). 

- **Bad Decision.** The GM will **add ticks** to a clock of their choice (an antagonistic faction’s clock, the ticking timebomb, etc.). 

- **Bender.** Your character vanishes for a few weeks. Play a different character until this one returns from their bender. When your character returns, they’ve also healed any harm they had. 

- **Brag** about your exploits. **+2 Heat.** 

- **Brawl** with people you shouldn’t have. Take **-1 status** with the faction they belong to _OR_ mark **level 2 harm** . 

- **Tapped.** Your current hookup cuts you off. Find a new source for your vice. 

165 

## **Long-Term Project** 

Even more than healing or relieving Stress, long-term projects are the most important downtime activity in the game. A long-term project can accomplish almost anything, from doing research into a paranormal phenomenon, investigating a mystery, establishing someone’s trust, gaining a new friend or contact, changing your character’s vice, and so on. 

When you work on a long-term project (either a brand-new one or an existing one), describe what your character does to advance the project clock, and roll one of your **actions** . 

Mark segments on the clock according to your result: 

|**1-3**|One segment|
|---|---|
|**4/5**|Two segments|
|**6**|Three segments|
|**Critical**|Five segments|



You can use a downtime activity to work on another PC’s long-term project. 

## **Inventing and Crafting** 

Invention, or crafting, is just another form of long-term project. Discuss how big the clock should be with the GM. Compare the Quality of the creation against the current Tier of the crew—as the crew increases in Tier, access to better materials and tools should make it easier to create complex or powerful inventions. 

- **4-clock** for a modification to an existing item. 

- **6-clock** for a useful gadget (relative to the crew’s Tier). 

- **Up to a 12-clock** for something really innovative and powerful (or even multiple clocks). 

_Don’t overthink it, just make a call._ Player and GM can negotiate—add drawbacks or compromises to reduce the clock size back down (e.g. conspicuous, unreliable, limited uses, etc.). If you reinvent something you’ve invented before, the clock size is one smaller. 

If the clock is at half or less when you take the downtime activity, you can work on an invention project with either **Study** or **Tinker** . If the clock is over halfway, you can only work on it with **Tinker** . 

Once the long-term project clock is complete, you have the invention. 

166 

Most inventions last forever (unless they break due to a consequence). Some inventions may have limited uses (often as a way to negotiate down the clock size). 

_Hattie invents some poison darts, and accepts only having six of them in exchange for a smaller invention clock._ 

## **Jury-Rigging** 

If you’re in a hurry, you can **jury-rig** an invention with one roll (no clock needed). Make a **Tinker** roll, and the result indicates the Quality of the invention relative to the crew’s Tier. 

1-3: Inferior (Tier -1), 4/5: Standard (Tier), 6: Fine (Tier +1), critical: Exceptional (Tier +2). 

## **A jury-rigged gadget only lasts one score.** 

## **Paranormal Experiments** 

The fringe science of the paranormal is not to be taken lightly. Unlike mundane invention, paranormal experimentation involves meddling with powerful and volatile forces. The rewards are great, but so are the risks. 

A paranormal experiment has two parts, **theory** and **performance** . 

The theory requires a **6-segment long-term project clock** . When the clock is complete, the player and the GM answer questions about the experiment to define what it will do in play and what is required to perform it. The player records these answers in their notes for future reference. 

## **Theory Questions:** 

**1.** GM asks: **“What does the experiment do, and how is it weird?”** Player answers. 

**2.** Player asks: **“What must I do to perform the experiment, and what is its price?”** GM answers. A paranormal experiment takes at least one downtime activity to perform and inflicts Stress on the experimenter according to its magnitude. If the experiment is dangerous or troublesome in some way, it requires an action roll (usually Attune). An experiment may also have additional costs, such as a sacrifice, rare items (alluvium, a large power source, secret data stored on punch cards, an ingredient from the deathlands), the start of a dire progress clock, Heat, investigation by Section 6, etc. 

**3.** GM asks: **“What’s the worst thing that could happen if this experiment goes bad?”** Player answers. 

167 

## **Performing the Experiment** 

To perform an experiment, you must have the **Forbidden Science** special ability, then follow the method outlined by the answers to the theory questions. Most experiments will take **one downtime activity** to complete, though the GM may call for two (or more) downtime activities for very powerful or far-reaching experiments. Some experiments may be partially performed during downtime and then fully manifested at will later by completing some final catalyst action. In this case, simply make a note that the experiment has been “primed” and may be unleashed at a later time. 

When you perform an experiment, you take an amount of **Stress** as established by the experiment questions, according to the magnitude of the forces brought to bear. The GM uses magnitude as a guideline for setting the Stress cost—it may be higher or lower at their discretion to better describe the nature of the experiment. 

Here are some guidelines: 

- **2 Stress** is the same as pushing yourself. This is a minor cost. Choose this amount if the experiment feels like something they should be able to do fairly easily. 

- **4 Stress** is about half the Stress meter. It’s a reasonable cost, but can be reversed with a typical Indulge Vice downtime activity. Choose this amount if you want them to be able to do the experiment every score, but not without paying a meaningful price. 

- **6+ Stress** is a lot. Choose this amount if you want the experiment to be something they save for when they really need it. 

_Paranormal experiments are fun and should be encouraged_ — _lean toward lower Stress costs to ensure they actually get used at the table._ 

Experiments take time to perform. Use the duration examples on the magnitude table to reduce the Stress cost based on the time needed, generally no less than an hour. 

The GM may also **tick a progress clock** when you conduct an experiment—to advance the agenda of a paranormal entity, or to show the steady approach of a dark outcome that is a consequence of the experiment. 

If an experiment is dangerous or troublesome to perform, make an **action roll** (usually **Attune** ) to see if unpleasant consequences manifest. If an experiment has an uncertain effect, then a **fortune roll** should be made to see how well it manifests. Because a ritual is a downtime activity, you may spend **stacks** 1-for-1 to increase the result level of your fortune roll (this represents the expenditure of expensive or rare materials or ingredients). If an experiment is both dangerous and uncertain, then both rolls may be called for. 

168 

Each performance of an experiment is a unique event, and may not always work the same way each time. The GM or players may call for a round of theory questions to establish an experiment anew. 

## **Recover** 

When you Recover, you seek treatment and heal your harm. You might visit a doctor who can stitch your wounds and soothe your mind with modern medicine, or a healer who specializes in chanting and herbal remedies. If you don’t have a contact or fellow PC who can provide treatment, you can use the **Acquire Asset** activity to gain access to a healer, who can provide service for the whole crew. 

Recovery is like a long-term project. Your healer rolls ( **Tinker** for a PC with the Anatomist special ability, or the **Quality** level of an NPC) and then you mark a number of segments on your **healing clock** . 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0179-05.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1-3 One segment (and clear all level 1 harm)<br>4/5 Two segments (and clear all level 1 harm)<br>6 Three segments (and clear all level 1 harm)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


> **Critical** Five segments (and clear all level 1 harm) 

When you fill your healing clock, reduce each instance of harm on your sheet by one level, then clear the clock. If you have more segments to mark, they “roll over.” 

Taking the Recover downtime activity at all, no matter the result, clears all level 1 harm. 

You may heal yourself if you have the **Anatomist** special ability, but you take 2 Stress when you do so. You can also choose to simply tough it out and attempt to heal without any treatment—in this case, take 1 Stress and roll 0d. 

Note that it’s the recovering character that takes the Recover action. Healing someone else does not cost a downtime activity for the healer. 

## **Hospital** 

Instead of finding your own medical treatment, you can use the Recover activity to stay in a hospital. **Clear all your harm.** 

- If you go **public** , the crew takes **3 Heat** . 

- If you go **private** , it costs **3 stacks** . 

169 

## **Hull Repair** 

Hulls cannot go to the hospital or use a regular healer. They have three options for repair: 

- **Acquire Asset** to gain access to a sparktech or similar. 

- Let another PC try their **Tinker** (no special ability required). The Hull uses a downtime activity, not the other PC. 

- Let your controlling faction repair you. Tick the “Suspicion” clock once, and clear all harm. 

## **Reduce Heat** 

Say what your character does to reduce the **Heat** level of the crew and make an action roll. Maybe you **Consort** with your friend who’s a Bluecoat and she arranges for a few incriminating Watch reports to disappear. Or maybe you **Command** the fear of the local citizens so they’re afraid to snitch. 

Reduce Heat according to the result: 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0180-09.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1-3 -1 Heat<br>4/5 -2 Heat<br>6 -3 Heat<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


> **Critical** -5 Heat 

## **Acquire Asset** 

Gain temporary use of an asset. An asset could be: 

- One special **item** , or a set of common items (enough for a gang of your Tier scale). 

- A **cohort** (an expert or gang). 

- A **vehicle** . 

- A **service** . Transport from a smuggler or driver, use of a warehouse for temporary storage, legal representation, etc. 

**To acquire the asset, roll the crew’s Tier** , with bonus dice equal to your personal **lifestyle quality** (full rows of bank). The result indicates the Quality of the asset you get. 

170 

> **1-3** Quality equals **Tier -1** 

> **4/5** Quality equals **Tier** 

> **6** Quality equals **Tier +1** 

> **Critical** Quality equals **Tier +2** 

The cost of the asset is assumed to be covered by your small change, walkingaround money, favors owed, etc. Spending stacks to improve the roll result means it requires a significant outlay of hard cash. 

You can spend stacks to raise the result of this roll beyond critical by spending 2 stacks per additional Tier level added. 

## **You Always Get Something** 

If you fail to reach the required Quality level, either accept a lesser version (unreliable or flawed in some way), or create a clock equal to the required Quality level and add ticks equal to the result you _did_ achieve. Consider it a down payment, and you’ll get the asset when the clock is full. 

If you acquire the same asset again, you get +1d to your roll. 

Firearms, poisons, explosives, and dangerous gadgets are highly restricted in Doskvol. When you use Acquire Asset to get one of these items (rather than inventing it yourself), you take +2 Heat. 

## **The Goods Last Until They’re Gone** 

Goods acquired don’t last for one score, they last until they’re gone. Exactly how long that is will depend on the context, but if you purchased a piece of equipment, it lasts until the fiction says it’s used up or destroyed. If it’s a service (e.g. doctor) or cohort, it probably lasts a few scores. You could create a clock to track the service’s use (perhaps four segments). 

If you want to gain an asset permanently, you can either choose it as a crew upgrade (using the rules for advancement) or work on it as a long-term project to set up a permanent playbook item. 

## **Training Montage** 

When you spend time in training, **mark 1 xp** on the xp track for an **attribute** or **playbook** advancement. If you have the appropriate crew Training upgrade unlocked, mark **+1 xp** (2 total). You can train a given xp track only once per downtime. 

171 

## **Sample Inventions** 

**Bulletproof Briefcase** Quality I 6-clock 

A leather briefcase with bulletproof lining. Can be used once per score to resist one instance of harm entirely. The harm must be something you could reasonably block with a bulletproof briefcase. **0 load.** 

**Drawbacks:** Once used, requires one downtime activity to repair before it can be used again. 

**Silence Field** Quality III 6-clock 

A handheld gadget that generates a room-sized zone of complete silence. **1 load. Drawbacks:** Only has enough power for one use per score, lasting a few minutes. 

**Voice Modulator** Quality II 6-clock 

A small gadget that modifies your voice to disguise your identity. Can mimic a specific individual if the modulator has first been used to record a few sentences of them speaking. **0 load.** 

**Auto Lockpick** Quality III 6-clock 

A spider-like gadget that can imprint itself on any mechanical lock and open it within seconds. **0 load.** 

**Drawbacks:** Can only be used once per score—it configures itself to the lock it opens, and it takes at least an hour to reset it. 

**Laser Cutter** Quality II 8-clock 

A handheld device that emits a precise laser beam capable of quietly cutting through solid brick or metal. **1 load.** 

**Drawbacks:** The laser cutter is slow, and cutting through particularly hard or thick material could take several minutes (or even longer, if a large hole is required). 

172 

**Hologram Projector** Quality III 8-clock 

Casts a person-sized 3D image in vivid detail. **1 load.** 

**Drawbacks:** Unreliable—when you use the item, make a fortune roll (using its Quality) to see how well it performs. 

**Jetpack** Quality IV 8-clock 

A backpack-sized personal propulsion device with twin thrusters. **2 load. Drawbacks:** Limited fuel—may run low, or run out, as a consequence of rolls. 

**Freeze Ray** Quality V 10-clock 

Drops the target’s temperature below freezing and materializes solid ice around them, freezing them in place. Powered by alluvium. **1 load.** 

**Drawbacks:** Alluvium tech draws attention from Section 6—take **+1 Heat** if the item was used any number of times during a score. 

**Human Amplifier** Quality VI 10-clock 

A bulky exoskeleton that enhances the wearer’s strength, allowing them to lift heavy objects and perform superhuman feats. Choose whether it’s designed for combat or manual labor. **Loud approach only—wearer can take +1 load.** 

**War Amplifier:** The user’s baseline strength and reflexes are improved. This factors into effect for actions involving athletics, close combat, firearms, etc. Once per score, the rig can be supercharged for a feat of superhuman strength or agility. 

**Work Amplifier:** Includes multiple mechanical arms, enabling the user to manipulate several objects at once. The user’s baseline strength is improved, and they can perform mechanical work or manual labor at increased speed and efficiency. This factors into effect. Once per score, the rig can be supercharged for a feat of superhuman manual efficiency. 

**Drawbacks:** If the human amplifier shuts down due to a consequence of failed rolls etc., the wearer becomes immobilized until they can unstrap themselves from the contraption. If they wish to carry it around underpowered, it requires 2 load. 

173 

## **Shopping Catalog (Acquire Asset Examples)** 

## **Bulletproof Vest.** 2 load. 

- **Quality 0:** One use. Resists one instance of relevant harm by one step. 

- **Quality 1:** Two uses. Each use reduces one instance of relevant harm by one step. 

- Each additional level of Quality adds another use (e.g. a Quality 4 bulletproof vest would have five uses), and/or may reduce load required. 

## **Rebreather.** 1 load. 

- **Quality 0:** Allows the user to breathe underwater and protects them from breathable gases, toxins, etc. Lasts for one scene only. 

- **Quality 1:** Usable for one score. 

- **Quality 2:** Reusable until it’s damaged or destroyed by a consequence from a failed roll etc. Higher Quality levels protect against more dangerous gases and toxins, and/or may reduce load required. 

## **Parachute.** 

- **Quality 0:** 2 load, one use only, unreliable (fortune roll required). 

- **Quality 1:** 2 load, one use only. 

- **Quality 2:** 1 load, one use only. 

- **Quality 3:** 0 load, one use only. 

- **Quality 4:** Compact deployable glider. 1 load, reusable until destroyed. 

- **Quality 5:** Compact deployable glider. 0 load, reusable until destroyed. 

**Bugging Kit.** Wiretaps and recording devices for surveillance. 

- **Quality 0:** 2 load. One format only (e.g. phone tap, live sound recording, or black and white camera). Unreliable (fortune roll required). 

- **Quality 1:** 2 load. Two formats. Unreliable (fortune roll required). 

- **Quality 2:** 1 load. Two formats. 

- **Quality 3:** 1 load. Multiple formats. 

- **Quality 4:** 0 load. Multiple formats. 

174 

**Sniper’s Nest.** Rent a room in an adjacent building for the perfect vantage point. 

- **Quality 0:** Time limit on how long you have the place to yourself. Imperfect view of the target (fortune roll required at any given moment for line of sight). Unreliable landlord or nosy neighbors—they may rat you out as a consequence of failed rolls. 

- **Quality 1:** Time limit on how long you have the place to yourself. Imperfect view of the target (fortune roll required at any given moment for line of sight). 

- **Quality 2:** Time limit on how long you have the place to yourself. 

- **Quality 3:** _No drawbacks._ 

- **Quality 4+:** Includes some advantage, such as a discrete emergency exit. 

**Military Grade Weaponry.** Take +2 Heat whenever you acquire these items. The Quality simply reflects the current condition of the weapon, and is compared to the Tier of any enemy it is used against. 

- **Sniper rifle** 

- **Grenade launcher** 

- **Rocket launcher** 

- **Heat blade** 

- **Light Machine Coilgun (LMC)** 

- **Heavy Assault Coilgun (HAC50)** 

**Cohorts.** Experts or gangs can be temporarily hired using Acquire Asset. Note down the Quality rolled, which is used for any fortune rolls required when the cohort takes action. Typically, such a cohort will stick around for four uses. A use might include one score, one Recover downtime activity, the cohort taking a cohort downtime activity, etc. You might decide that negative consequences, such as a gang taking harm, counts as an extra “use.” You can trade down the Quality of the cohort in exchange for more uses. 

- **Lawyer** (reducing Heat) 

- **Doctor** (healing, recovery) 

- **Forger** (producing fake IDs, passports, documents) 

- **Codebreaker** (bypassing security systems) 

- **Charming Attaché** (a well-dressed assistant skilled in social encounters) 

- **Body Double** (a lookalike to attend in your place or cause confusion; very useful when combined with flashbacks) 

- **Personal Stylist** (factors into effect when looking staggeringly impressive will help) 

175 

## **Sample Paranormal Experiments** 

Every paranormal experiment is unique and unpredictable, so use the following examples as inspiration only. If you do use one of these, make sure to go over the theory questions for yourself and fill in the missing gaps (such as how the experiment is performed). 

## **Containment Tether** 

Binds a captured resonance entity to its containment unit by a tether. The entity can be released and then instantly pulled back into the unit at the experimentalist’s command. _What is the nature of the tether? Is it an unseen force, or does it have a physical component such as a cable, wire, or arc of electroplasm?_ 

**Price:** 2 Stress for an echo, 4 Stress for an aberration, 7 Stress for a Class VI Metadimensional Entity. A fortune roll determines how strong the tether is and how long it will last for (a few minutes, half an hour, or several hours). The tether is good for one use and must then be renewed with a fresh experiment. _What other price is required? Perhaps a particular material such as a length of gold wire, or perhaps you have to tell the entity a personal secret every time you perform the experiment._ 

**Dangers** : A bond is not easily broken. If the entity were to ever escape, it could use the lingering tether to pull the paranormalist to it, just as it was once pulled to the containment unit. 

## **Symbiotic Fusion** 

Two willing participants are bound together. When triggered during a score, the two participants merge into one individual for a single scene. They must collaborate and agree on the actions that the resultant individual takes. It has access to special abilities and equipment from either party, and uses the best rating of either for action rolls (if merged with an NPC, use their Tier to determine Quality). _How is the merge triggered? Is it activated by a worn device, a code phrase, an arcane pattern, or some kind of fusion dance?_ 

**Price:** 4 Stress to the experimentalist. Both participants take level 1 harm (Shock) when the fusion ends. 

**Dangers** : There is a possibility that things may get muddled—action ratings, special abilities, memories, or personality traits may get swapped over. 

176 

A strange and unique outcome is always a good place to start for a paranormal experiment, but if you’re struggling to think of one, another approach is to consider the kind of help you want during a score and work backward from there. Some ideas are provided here. 

Going through the theory question will help bring these dry categories to life. The theory questions will also color in who your paranormalist is—one paranormalist’s experiments might involve arcane rituals with a sacred dagger and goat’s blood, while another’s might involve strange machines and a long formula scrawled on a chalkboard, and a third’s might be a strange combination of the two. 

**Freeze an individual.** It’s often useful to put a target out of action for a while, whether to remove them as an obstacle, stop them causing a fuss, or transport them safely. 

- _Is this a time-stasis contraption, or a ritual that literally freezes the target in ice, or are they psychically stunned, slimed, or held by a summoned resonance entity?_ 

**Get past an obstacle.** Whether guards, a laser corridor, or a locked door, scoundrels frequently have to find a way to quickly move past an obstacle. 

- _Is this a time gadget (slow or speed up time), or temporary invisibility, or a portal through space, or psychic suggestion, or shifting into the resonance field to become physically intangible?_ 

**Enhance your abilities.** The paranormalist may not be the strongest, quickest, or most charismatic, but they can offset this with the right paranormal experiment. For this kind of thing, an Attune roll might stand in for another action. 

- _Perhaps you allow yourself to be temporarily possessed by an entity with useful skills (with a safety system to automatically eject the entity), or perhaps you manipulate gravity, time, probabilities, or emotions to get the result you want._ 

**Summon help.** An experiment might summon the equivalent of a cohort (expert or gang) to help out during a score. 

- _Do you animate objects (such as department store mannequins), or summon a resonance entity such as a ghost or gremlin, or create your own weird robot, or open a portal to another place, time, or dimension?_ 

177 

**Protection.** Perhaps you want a way to reduce or resist harm, whether physical or mental, to yourself or others (as always, the scope will determine the Stress cost). 

- _Is this some kind of protective force field, or a time loop to rewind the harm, or summoning a resonance entity to intercept the harm, or manifesting physical protection such as a wave of water, a magnetic field, or a wall of stone?_ 

**Make friends and influence people.** Without getting into the fraught area of mind control, there are other ways to make friends and win someone over. 

- _Do you increase your own charisma, tell people what they want to hear, or create an instant paranormal party time with music and mood lighting?_ 

178 

179 

## Planning Meeting 

Some time during personal business (usually at the end, but it could be at the beginning, or in the middle), the crew has a planning meeting to pick their next score. Follow the steps in this section. 

## 1. Sketch opportunities 

Each player, as well as the GM, provides **one** of: A **client** , or a **target** , or an **operation type** . 

## 2. Roll for payouts 

Roll dice equal to your **crew Tier** , and **add the Tier of the target** to the result. This is the payout in stacks. Bigger payouts mean bigger scores. 

## 3. Choose the next score 

All the players agree on the next primary score. They may also choose a secondary score for a crew cohort to attempt. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0190-09.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Keri<br>SCORPION syndicate<br>Red Torque (2)<br>6 stacks<br>OPPORTUNITY<br>From :<br>Client :<br>Target :<br>Type :<br>:<br>Payout :<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


180 

## **1. Sketch opportunities** 

Each player, as well as the GM, sketches out an opportunity. Provide **one** of either a **client** , or a **target** , or an **operation type** . 

- **Client:** Perhaps you want to improve your status with a faction you’ve pissed off, or perhaps you want to work toward cementing an ally. 

- **Target:** Perhaps you want to hinder an enemy faction who are rising in Tier, or get payback on a faction who wronged you. 

- **Operation type:** A preferred operation, a claim, or something that accomplishes a particular goal or adds/removes segments from a particular clock (such as ending a war, dealing with ongoing trouble, or slowing a ticking timebomb). Could also be something as broad as “a weird paranormal score.” 

Write your choice down on an index card. 

At this stage, we’re just presenting the rough outline of an opportunity, which is why we only choose one of the above. It makes the process faster and prevents the indecision of trying to craft the perfect opportunity. It’s fine to write in more details if they already exist in the fiction (sometimes you’ll present an opportunity you heard about during personal business). 

You don’t have to justify the opportunity, just pick something that interests you, either as a player or as your character. Consider what might be best for your character, for the crew’s development, or even just what seems interesting or funny in the moment. 

_Keri is interested in getting friendly with the SCORPION Syndicate, so he puts down an opportunity where they’re the client faction._ 

_The GM has noticed the players haven’t engaged with the claim map yet, so they put down “Specialist Fence claim,” mostly just to remind the players that they can go for claims._ 

_The others have already chosen some strategic opportunities, so Olivia decides to pick something fun. She writes down “something at the fireworks factory.”_ 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0191-12.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
OPPORTUNITY<br>From : GM<br>Client :<br>Target :<br>Type : Claim (specialist fence)<br>:<br>Payout :<br>From :<br>Client :<br>Target :<br>Type :<br>:<br>Payout :<br>OPPORTUNITY<br>Keri<br>SCORPION syndicate<br>From :<br>Client :<br>Target :<br>Type :<br>:<br>Payout :<br>OPPORTUNITY<br>Olivia<br>Fireworks Factory<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


181 

## **2. Roll for payouts** 

For every opportunity on the table, **roll dice equal to your crew Tier, and add the Tier of the target** to the result. 

If the target isn’t a particular faction, or you haven’t defined the target yet, the GM will supply the target’s Tier (it’s fine for the GM to pick any arbitrary Tier, and find a faction that fits later). 

The result tells you how many **stacks** that opportunity pays out. The higher the total result, the more difficult the job. 

## **Payout** 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0192-06.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
|||
|---|---|
|2|A minor job. Should be fairly short and easy.|
|4|A small job.|
|6|A standard score. Moderate challenge.|
|8|A big score. Difficult.|
|10+|A major score. Very difficult. Expect multiple deadlocks, major harm, etc.|

**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


The difficulty of a score is flexible, and should be relative to your own tastes in score complexity, session length, and so on. Go off instinct for your first score, and then try to size future scores by that yardstick. If other factors (such as claim opportunities) modify the total payout, they don’t affect the difficulty of the score. 

## **Claim Opportunities** 

A claim provides significant benefits of its own, so if the score will result in a new **claim** for the crew, the GM can consider **subtracting 0 to 3 stacks** from the total payout. The claim is a large part of the reward. If the payout does go down, the difficulty remains the same. 

## **Never subtract from the payout for one-off opportunities on the claim map.** 

At the start of a campaign, do not subtract any stacks from the payout for claims. As your campaign progresses, keep an eye on how fast the crew is advancing along the campaign map, and how long you want your campaign to last. Applying a payout penalty to claims will act as a handbrake, slowing their progress. 

If the crew takes a claim every score, that means they could access the big one-off opportunity at the end of their claim map in as little as 7 scores (but they’ll probably take a bit longer; it’s unlikely they’ll go straight to it). This isn’t a bad thing, but if you want a longer campaign, make claims a bit harder to get. 

182 

## **Dealers and Product Units** 

For a crew of Dealers, some or all of the payout may be product units instead of stacks. If so, add **+2 units** on top of the conversion. 

_Tova has rolled a Bulk Acquisition opportunity with a 7 stack payout. Since this is about acquiring product, the GM decides that 4 of the stacks will be product units instead, and adds +2 units on top for the conversion. The final payout is 6 product units (4 stacks converted to units, with a +2 bonus), as well as the remaining 3 stacks of cash._ 

## **Bonus Payout** 

You can add stacks to the payout if you think the situation merits it. 

Instead of adding a bonus payout up front, consider having options within the score for extra payout (e.g. valuables that could be stolen, over and above the target of the score), or Called Shots (see next section). 

Write the final payout under the opportunity. 

_Olivia rolls for the payout for her fireworks factory idea. The PC crew is Tier 1, so she rolls one die and gets a 3. She asks the GM what Tier the target is for this opportunity. The GM has a quick look at the faction sheet, and chooses one they have a history with—the Limmerfield Gang, Tier 2. The payout for this score is 5 (3 + 2), which Olivia writes on her index card._ 

_The GM rolls next, for their claim opportunity. They roll one die and get a 5. They haven’t decided who the target will be yet, but decides they’ll be Tier 3. If the players pick this opportunity, the GM will decide a specific target faction between sessions. The payout is 8 stacks (5 + 3). Since part of the payout for this score is a claim, the GM could subtract some stacks, but decides not to; they want to encourage the players to get some claims._ 

_Keri rolls a 4 for the opportunity with the SCORPION Syndicate as clients. The GM looks up their faction entry and decides the target will be one of their enemies; Red Torque, Tier 2. The payout is 6 stacks._ 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0193-11.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
OPPORTUNITY<br>From : Olivia<br>Client :<br>Target : Limmerfield Gang (2)<br>OPPORTUNITY<br>Type : Fireworks Factory<br>From : GM :<br>Client : Payout : 5 stacks<br>Target : Tier 3<br>Type : Claim (specialist fence)<br>:<br>Payout : 8 stacks + claim<br>From :<br>Client :<br>Target :<br>Type :<br>:<br>Payout :<br>Keri<br>SCORPION syndicate<br>Red Torque (2)<br>6 stacks<br>OPPORTUNITY<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


183 

## **3. Choose the Next Score** 

Consider the opportunities on the table, their scope, and their payouts. Debate the merits, then pick one that you all agree on. That’s your next score. The players and the GM can work together to define missing details (the client, target, or operation type, as applicable—the GM has final say). Not every score has to have a client or a target faction (it must have a target Tier, but this doesn’t have to point at a specific faction). 

If you have any crew cohorts, one can attempt a **secondary score** from the opportunities left on the table. They’ll tackle this off-screen while the PCs handle the primary score. See page 96 for details. 

Once you’ve chosen a score, you might flashback to the scene where you arranged it with the client to get a bit more context. This can also be a good place to end the session, so the GM has time to flesh out the details between sessions. When you’re ready, it’s time for the score. 

_With all three opportunities on the table, the players lean back and talk about their options. They first compare the scope of each—with payouts of 5 and 6, Keri and Olivia’s opportunities are at the small/standard end of the spectrum. At 8 stacks plus a claim, the GM’s opportunity will be a big, difficult score._ 

_The players debate the merits of each, but ultimately the Crowfoot Chancers have a reputation of “ambitious” and they’re not here to play it safe. They decide that they’ll tackle the big claim score._ 

_The crew has a cohort; a five-person gang. They could bring them on the primary score as backup, but they decide to let them prove themselves on a secondary mission. They agree that the gang’s type (Infiltrators) could apply to either of the remaining opportunities. They choose the one with the smallest scope; their gang will attempt the fireworks factory as the secondary score._ 

## **Holding Opportunities** 

As long as it makes sense in the fiction, scores that weren’t picked can be saved for the next planning meeting—you can put the same one forward again, along with the agreed payout, instead of supplying a new one. Think of each opportunity as taking one player’s slot (including the GM). Each planning meeting should end with one opportunity for each slot. It’s fine to hold an opportunity for as long as it makes sense to. 

Alternatively, you can propose the same opportunity but reroll the payout. You don’t have to think of a new opportunity every single planning meeting—only if your opportunity was used, or the GM decides it’s no longer applicable, or you decide to withdraw it. 

184 

_When the next planning meeting comes around, the GM and Olivia’s opportunities have been used and they’ll need to sketch out some new ones. Keri’s SCORPION Syndicate opportunity is still on the table. He considers replacing it but can’t really be bothered, so he leaves it for another round._ 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0195-02.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
OPPORTUNITY<br>From : Keri<br>Client : SCORPION syndicate OPPORTUNITY<br>Target : Red Torque (2) From : GM<br>Type : Client :<br>: Target :<br>Payout : 6 stacks Type :<br>:<br>Payout :<br>OPPORTUNITY<br>From : Olivia<br>Client :<br>Target :<br>Type :<br>:<br>Payout :<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **The Planning Meeting Isn’t Necessary** 

Sometimes it’s obvious what the next score will be (like when you unlock a score opportunity on the claim map, or when you’re at war and there’s a pressing matter to deal with), or sometimes you stumble into a score during personal business. If you all agree, you can skip the planing meeting and jump straight to the obvious score. Make an engagement roll and go. 

## **The GM Can Still Prep for the Score** 

If the GM likes to have time to prepare, move things around to ensure that the planning meeting happens at the end of a session. This could mean halfway through personal business—that’s fine. Choose the score at the end of the session, then conclude personal business at the start of the next session before moving on to the agreed score. 

The more you play, the better you’ll be able to estimate how long your scores take, how much you get through in a typical session, and how to pace things so the planning meeting happens at a time that’s convenient for you. The important part is that the PCs choose their own opportunities; it’s not important whether the GM improvises the score or not. 

Alternatively, if you don’t like prep and prefer to make up challenges on the fly, go right ahead! 

_The GM doesn’t mind a bit of improvisation but they want to have a few details figured out before the score starts. After the players pick the claim score, the GM suggests they end the session, and they do end-of-session xp. Between sessions, the GM thinks about the score a little bit and decides who the target is (whoever currently has the claim), as well as a few ideas of where the score will take place, surprising moments, potential obstacles, and so on._ 

185 

## **Called Shots** 

Called Shots are an optional mechanic that lets the crew take on challenging extra objectives to increase the payout for a score. 

You might like to introduce the option after a few scores. If there’s an opportunity that the crew really wants to pursue but they wish it had a higher payout, Called Shots are a way to get there. 

Some examples are shown here, but consider the specifics of the score to tailor your own Called Shots. These discussions could happen between sessions if the GM wants to do some prep first. 

A Called Shot should add **+1** – **3 stacks** to the payout, depending on the score and the capabilities of the crew. The more challenging it seems, the higher the bonus payout should be. **Failing a Called Shot subtracts the same amount from the payout** , as a penalty. 

Either the GM or the players can propose Called Shots, but the GM has final say (it’s up to the players whether they accept—just like a Devil’s Bargain). 

_The Crowfoot Chancers have selected an opportunity to get a claim on the claim map—Specialist Fence, which would give them +2 stacks for future heists or subterfuge._ 

_The GM has already declared the target will be Tier 3, but didn’t pick a faction at the time. Between sessions, they look at the faction list for some options. They feel like the players should still have a choice, so they message the group chat. “There are two factions with a specialist fence you could go after; the Tomorrow Program, who you currently have -1 status with, or the Golden Hand, who you haven’t dealt with before (they’re hightech criminals with a bunch of hulls). Which one do you want to go after?”_ 

_The players decide not to antagonize yet another new faction, and stick with the devil they know—they’ll target the Tomorrow Program. The GM reads their faction description and flicks through the book for some other ideas._ 

_At the start of the next session, they outline the opportunity in more detail. “Duckworth, the concierge at Dundridges department store in Nightmarket, is a well-connected and experienced fence, and currently affiliated with the Tomorrow Program...”_ 

_After a little more briefing, the GM offers two Called Shots. If they avoid any violence, they’ll get +1 stack payout (paid by Duckworth, as thanks for keeping this business discrete). Additionally, a very expensive emerald_ 

186 

_necklace is currently on display in the jewelery department of Dundridges. It could be stolen for +2 stacks._ 

_The players don’t want their hands tied behind their back, so they reject the first Called Shot about no violence. Stealing a necklace sounds like their bag though, so they accept the second one. If they fail to steal the necklace, 2 stacks will instead be subtracted from the payout._ 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0197-03.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1 – 2<br>1 Steal an unrelated item nearby.<br>2 Plant a device while you’re there.<br>3 Frame someone else for the crime.<br>4 Do it within a set time limit.<br>5 Make it look like an accident or act of nature.<br>6 Don’t kill anyone, or avoid any violence completely.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>



![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0197-04.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
3 – 4<br>1 Make it so nobody notices the crime has occurred (for a while, at least).<br>2 Burn the place down when you leave.<br>3 Make it happen in a specific venue (e.g. in the local Bluecoat precinct).<br>4 Do it without raising the alarm.<br>5 Make it loud and obvious to send a message.<br>6 Capture or free a specific resonance entity.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>



![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0197-05.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
5 – 6<br>1 Successfully start a rumor while you’re there.<br>2 Create a scandal.<br>3 Do it without lying.<br>4 Make it look like you, or the client, are heroes.<br>5 Leave the target thinking the score was their idea all along.<br>6 Create a rift between two key NPCs.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


187 

## The Score 

Whether selected through the planning meeting (page 180) or simply something the characters fall into, a score is a single operation with a particular goal. A score can be long and involved or short and sweet. 

Before the score begins, PCs might choose to gather some information ( _Blades in the Dark,_ page 36). Then they choose an approach and make an engagement roll. 

The GM then cuts to the score in progress. The PCs engage the target— they make rolls, overcome obstacles, call for flashbacks, and complete the operation (successfully or not). 

## **Approach** 

When the PCs feel ready (or even better, before they feel ready), they choose an **approach** for the score. 

There are six different approaches (see the following list). The chosen approach doesn’t lock you into any particular plan or type of score—it only indicates the nature of your initial approach, and informs the **engagement roll** . What happens from there is up to you. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0198-08.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Assault Opening gambit:  The attack point<br>Deception Opening gambit:  The misdirection or disguise<br>Stealth Opening gambit:  The entry point<br>Paranormal Opening gambit:  The strange power<br>Social Opening gambit:  The social connection<br>Speed Opening gambit:  The moment of impact<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Opening Gambit** 

Choose an opening gambit; the vector of your approach. This makes your approach more specific, which helps the GM decide what the engagement roll result means: _will your opening gambit succeed perfectly, or is there some obstacle there?_ 

_It’s easy to get hung up on this, but it’s really just deciding what the first obstacle might look like—is it kicking in the front doors, or breaking in through a skylight, or something else?_ 

188 

## **Loadout** 

Finally, choose your loadout: **quiet** or **loud** . 

- If you go **quiet** , you each have **3 boxes of load** available. 

- If you go **loud** , you each have **6 boxes of load** available. 

This choice will also inform the engagement roll, and might impact the Heat you take. 

## **Engagement Roll** 

Once the players have chosen an approach, opening gambit, and loadout, make an engagement roll ( _Blades in the Dark_ , page 128). 

189 

## Aftermath 

## **Payout, Rep, and faction status changes** 

These are handled the same as standard _Blades in the Dark_ (page 146). 

## **Tithe** 

As part of payout, the crew is expected to **pay stacks equal to their crew Tier +1** as a tithe to their ward boss. If they choose not to pay, add one tick to the 4-segment “Anger” clock. Every time it fills up, the crew loses 1 faction status with them, and there may be other reprisals. 

## **Heat** 

After a score (or a raid), your crew takes Heat according to the nature of the operation. If the crew went **loud** with their loadout, consider this factor alongside everything else. 

**0 Heat:** Smooth & quiet; low exposure. 

**2 Heat:** Contained; standard exposure. 

**4 Heat:** Loud & chaotic; high exposure. 

**6 Heat:** Wild; devastating exposure. 

In addition: 

- Add **extra Heat** for a high-profile or well-connected target. This might be +1 Heat if the target is mid-Tier, or you only mildly pissed them off. It might be +3 Heat if they’re very high Tier and you really angered them. 

- Add **+1 Heat** if the situation happened on hostile turf. 

- Add **+1 Heat** if you’re at war with another faction. 

- Add **+2 Heat** if killing was involved (whether the crew did the killing or not— bodies draw attention). 

- Add **+1 Heat** if explosives were used. 

- Add **+1 Heat** if military-grade weapons were used. 

## **Imperial Mandate** 

With the right combination of abilities and claims, the crew might permanently stay at zero Heat, and never gain a Wanted Level. This might strain your suspension of disbelief. If their crimes are high profile (which they probably are if the crew is gaining Rep and going up in Tier), _someone_ in power will notice, 

190 

and the authorities will authorize a **Mandate** . It’s one thing to bamboozle the local Bluecoats, but throwing off a Mandate is another matter. 

A Mandate is a task force lead by an Inspector, with some Bluecoats assigned to the detail. The goal of a Mandate is to uncover and take down a criminal conspiracy. 

A Mandate is represented by a clock, typically 8 or 10 segments. Unless they have the _Cat and Mouse_ ability (Dealers crew), you do not have to tell the players they are the subject of a Mandate. It’s still worth putting the clock on the table so they know something’s brewing, but you don’t have to label it. 

**Every time the crew complete a criminal score, tick the clock.** You can also tick it if they do something particularly brazen or incriminating. _Hint at the Mandate’s activities during personal business; someone in an unmarked autopod taking photos of the crew, the faint click of a wiretap during a phone call, the sense of being followed in the street, and so on._ 

When the clock is complete, the crew **gains a number of Wanted Levels equal to their Tier** , immediately. 

The Reduce Heat downtime activity does not effect a Mandate’s clock, but long-term projects or targeted scores could do. 

_A Mandate is a heavy-handed approach that might feel unfair to the players. It’s best used only when the PCs are completely Heat-proof, and even then with caution._ 

## **Persons of Interest** 

Doskvol is a hotbed of espionage activity; another consideration for the GM is whether the crew’s activities have come to the attention of one or more spy agencies. 

Like a Mandate, this is a way of complicating the crew’s lives even if they’re evading all the Heat. Consider faction status changes with Blackthorn Park, the Palace, or Anixis Field Unit, even if those factions are not directly involved in the score—simply being a high-profile criminal group pulling off big scores is enough to gain their attention. They may identify the crew as a threat to be neutralized, or a potential asset to be manipulated—or both. 

191 

## **Harm Complications** 

Each PC rolls for any harm complications. Roll a number of dice equal to your total amount of harm. 

_Pendleton has two instances of level 1 harm, and one instance of level 2 harm. They roll 4d (1d + 1d + 2d) for harm complications during the score aftermath._ 

Take the highest result and consult the following table that corresponds to your **highest level** of harm. Hulls roll on the Hull tables. 

_Pendleton’s highest harm is level 2, so they use the second table, Level 2 Harm Complications._ 

Harm rules are on page 152. 

## **Level 1 Harm Complications** 

> **1-3 No complication** 

> **4/5 Shaken:** Take **-1d** to the **engagement** roll for the next score this character goes on. 

- **6 Fatigued:** Your character has **-1 load** available to them on the next score (whether quiet or loud). 

> **Crit Self-Medication:** This character **must** use one of their downtime activities to **Indulge Vice** during the personal business phase. They take **-1d** the first time they do. 

192 

## **Level 2 Harm Complications** 

- **1-3 No complication** 

- **4/5 Self-Medication:** This character **must** use one of their downtime activities to **Indulge Vice** during the personal business phase. They take **-1d** the first time they do. 

- **6 Condition Worsens:** Take an additional level 1 harm. 

- **Crit Impaired:** One of your highestrated **actions** is at **-1d** for the next score. Remove one dot temporarily (don’t forget to put it back after the score). 

## **Level 3 Harm Complications** 

- **1-3 Scarred:** Your injuries leave a permanent mark. What is it? 

- **4/5 Condition Worsens:** Take an additional level 1 harm. 

- **6 Impaired:** One of your highestrated **actions** is at **-1d** for the next score. Remove one dot temporarily (don’t forget to put it back after the score). 

- **Crit Critical Condition:** Either **go to the hospital** immediately during personal business (Recover downtime action), or **sit out the next score** and play a different character. 

## **Hull Damage Complications** 

## **Hull: Level 1** – **2 Damage Complications** 

> **1-3 No complication** 

- **4/5 No complication** 

- **6 Noisy:** Your frame starts making loud clanking noises intermittently. 

> **Crit Smoking and Sparking:** Take -1d to the engagement roll for the next score this character goes on. 

## **Hull: Level 3 Damage Complications** 

- **1-3 No complication** 

- **4/5 Smoking and Sparking:** Take -1d to the engagement roll for the next score this character goes on. 

- **6 Power Leak:** The next time you **recharge your capacitors** , **clear only 3 Drain** instead of 5 as normal. 

## **Crit Critical Condition:** Either 

- sit out the next score and play a different character as your owning faction keeps you for major repairs ( **clear all harm** and mark one tick on the **“Suspicion”** clock), or your **armor** is **not available** on the next score. 

193 

## **Unwind** 

The final step of the gameplay cycle is to unwind together, before the crew returns to personal business. A different player decides how the crew unwinds at the end of each aftermath. 

**Each PC clears 1 Stress.** _If they have the Quarters base upgrade, each PC also clears all level 1 harm._ Some unwind ideas are presented here. Pick, roll, or make up your own. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0204-04.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1 2 3<br>1 Go fishing Art gallery Karaoke<br>2 Group therapy Group argument Barhopping<br>3 Movie night Play cards Training routines<br>4 Plasmovision in the  Fondue in the  Play a board game<br>lounge conversation pit<br>5 Hit the club Dancing Big meal together<br>6 Walk in the park Picnic Driving aimlessly<br>4 5 6<br>1 Spa day Have fortunes told Book club<br>2 Roller skating  Get matching tattoos Trip on lysid together<br>3 Watch the fight   Attend a protest or rally Smoke echo weed<br>together together<br>4 Visit the fairground  Visit the flea market Play a friendly sports<br>match<br>5 Group meditation Go stargazing Sauna session<br>6 See a concert Visit a jazz bar Cry together<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Paired Unwind Scenes** 

After every PC has had one or two turns choosing an unwind scene, you could try some paired scenes. The PCs divide into groups of two (if there’s an odd number of PCs, the leftover one can unwind with a cohort or a friendly NPC of their choice). Each pair describes their own unwind scene together. This is another way to explore and expand the interrelationships between the crew members on screen. 

194 

## **Group Argument** 

It’s worth including at least one group argument in any long-running campaign. It’s cathartic, and it makes the strong found-family bonds that most crews have feel more believable and earned. Some players do not need any encouragement to dive into group arguments—if that’s the case, do carry on! 

Many other players feel uncomfortable with this kind of scene. You should not do one if any of the players don’t want to, but sometimes all they need is some encouragement and facilitation. If everyone is on board, you could try the following procedure. 

## **First, each player decides the thing their character is most likely to be** 

**criticized for.** The GM will often have the best birds-eye perspective on this, and having them offer ideas for these criticisms works well. As a starting point: 

- **Hound:** You need to get your act together. We all have baggage, but we don’t let it define us. 

- **Hull:** What’s your deal, anyway? We don’t really know who you are, or what you are, and we can’t really trust you. 

- **Intellectual:** We feel like you’re studying and manipulating us. You always act superior. 

- **Operative:** We can’t trust you; we don’t know where your true loyalties lie. 

- **Paranormalist:** Being hassled by Bluecoats is one thing, but your experimental meddling is going to get us all in deep, existential trouble. 

- **Radical:** You’re more dedicated to your revolution than the crew. 

- **Swinger:** You need to get skin in the game. You spend your time talking and schmoozing while we risk our lives. 

- **Veteran:** You’re too violent and/or too disciplined. This isn’t a military unit. 

Then, go through the following procedure, cycling through the PCs in any order. 

There’s no need for resolution at the end of the argument scene. Characters might iron it out during personal business or the next score, or they might not. Simmering resentment is good too. 

_First PC:_ Who says something, accidentally or in a passive aggressive way, that **needles** you? How do you needle them right back? 

_Second PC:_ How do you **overreact** ? What do you say, and to whom? 

_Then:_ **Escalation.** Someone says something that makes things personal and takes it up a level. Who? 

_And maybe:_ **Collateral damage.** Who do you fling mud at that wasn’t even involved in the argument so far? 

**Lash out.** How do you deliver an outburst? A fist on the table, something flung to the ground, or grabbing someone by the lapels? 

_Finally:_ **We’re done here.** Dramatic exits, doors slammed, awkward silence. 

195 

## SPECIAL PROCEDURES 

_These procedures provide guidance on how to deal with specific situations, like entanglements between sessions, or when a PC goes to prison. You don’t have to memorize these rules, or even read them, until the situation arises._ 

196 

## Trouble Engine 

Once per gameplay cycle, the GM should run the **Trouble Engine** between sessions. This involves updating faction clocks and rolling for new trouble (just like entanglements in core _Blades_ ). You might do this between gameplay cycles (after the aftermath and before personal business), but it’s flexible—do it whenever suits you best, and bring the trouble into play when you next have the chance. 

## 1. Faction Clocks 

Update relevant faction clocks based on events in play, or fortune rolls. 

## 2. Crew Trouble 

Check for entanglements targeted at the crew. 

## 3. Local Trouble 

Check for general new trouble in the local district. 

## 4. Escalations 

See if any ongoing troubles escalate. 

You don’t have to run the Trouble Engine every cycle, although it’s a good idea in the earlier stages of a campaign. Toward the end, you might find that you have so many complicated situations to attend to (so much organic trouble) that you don’t need the Trouble Engine. It’s fine to skip it. 

197 

## 1. Faction Clocks 

Update relevant faction clocks based on events in play, or fortune rolls. 

First, update faction clocks. Either advance the clocks however much feels right based on the fiction and the pacing of your campaign, or make a fortune roll using their Tier, modified up or down depending on the opposition or circumstances. Tick one segment for a 1-3 result, two segments for a 4/5 result, three segments for a 6 result, or five segments for a critical result. 

Each faction has suggested clocks in the Faction Rolodex (page 342). You don’t have to use these, but they give a good idea of that faction’s current goals and activities. Customize the clocks as needed to make them fit your campaign, ignore them, or create new ones. 

Don’t track every clock for every faction. Pick a few key clocks that seem relevant and interesting. 

## **The Ticking Timebomb** 

If you’re using a ticking timebomb (page 272), update this at the same time. As a general rule, you might tick it once every time you run the Trouble Engine (once per gameplay cycle). You could modify this up or down depending on events in the fiction, or your own campaign pacing. 

## 2. Crew Trouble 

Check for entanglements targeted at the crew. 

Next, check for crew troubles. These troubles are targeted specifically at the crew by other factions (often the law, but also payback from aggrieved enemies). 

- **Find the column that matches the crew’s current Heat level** . 

- Then **roll a number of dice equal to their Wanted Level** . 

If their Wanted Level is zero, roll two dice and keep the lowest result. This will tell you whether there’s no new trouble, a new light trouble, or a new heavy trouble. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0208-14.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Heat 0 – 3 Heat 4 – 5 Heat 6+<br>1-3 None 1-3 None 1-3 Light Trouble<br>4/5 None 4/5 Light Trouble 4/5 Light Trouble<br>6 Light Trouble 6 Heavy Trouble 6 Heavy Trouble<br>Crit Heavy Trouble Crit Heavy Trouble Crit Heavy Trouble<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


198 

If new trouble occurred, choose a result from the relevant table on this page, or roll randomly. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0209-02.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Light Crew Troubles<br>1–3 4–6<br>Wrong Place, Wrong Time Friendly Favor<br>1 1<br>Friend found badly beaten. A friendly faction demands a favor.<br>Gang Trouble Shakedown<br>2 One of your gangs causes  2 An acquaintance is forced to spill<br>trouble. what they know.<br>Debt (ongoing) Things Get Weird<br>3 A PC or one of their close  3 A Class VI Metadimensional Entity<br>friends is in the hole. approaches the crew.<br>Asking Around Thieving Bastards<br>4 4<br>Someone’s asking about you. Thieves target the crew’s base.<br>Under Investigation  Interrogation<br>5 (ongoing)  5 One of the PCs is taken in for<br>The crew is under surveillance. questioning.<br>Patrols (ongoing)  Rattling the Cage<br>6 The local district suffers  6 An unfriendly faction sends a gang<br>regular patrols. to make trouble.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Heavy Crew Troubles** 

**Flipped** **`1`** One of your contacts switches allegiance. **Challenged Claim** **`2`** Another faction makes a move against one of the crew’s claims. **Invasion** **`3`** An outside faction invades the local district. **Show of Force** **`4`** An enemy faction makes a play against the crew. **Assassination** **`5`** An enemy faction makes a lethal move against your crew. **Arrest** **`6`** The Bluecoats send a detail to arrest you. 

199 

## 3. Local Trouble 

Check for general new trouble in the local district. 

These are city troubles that aren’t usually targeted specifically at your crew, but that nevertheless make life more difficult. Start with **2d** for luck, and then: 

- If you have a **negative faction status** with the **citizenry** of your local district (where your base is located), take **-1d** . If you have a **positive faction status** with the **citizenry** of your local district, take **+1d** . 

- If any factions are at **war** in your local district, take **-1d** . 

- If any **faction clocks** have completed that seem like they would upset the status quo, take **-1d** . 

- If the crew has recently taken action to help with local problems, take **+1d** . 

## **Local Trouble** 

**1-3** Light Trouble **4/5** None, but take -1d to this roll next time **6** None A festival or celebration takes place in the local district, and any **Critical** PC who wishes to take part clears 1 Stress. 

Choose troubles from the list, or roll randomly. 

200 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0211-01.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
