201 

## 4. Escalations 

See if any ongoing troubles escalate. 

Next, check to see if any ongoing troubles escalate. If this is the first instance of the Trouble Engine, skip this step. 

## **Check for Escalations** 

Roll dice equal to half the number of ongoing troubles in play, rounded up. For example, if there are four troubles, roll 2d. If there are five troubles, roll 3d. 

- **1-3** No escalations 

- **4/5** One escalation **6** Two escalations 

- **Critical** Three escalations 

## **Define New Escalations** 

Escalations can be linked to any of the ongoing troubles, and can take a number of forms. Don’t escalate new troubles. 

To start, follow the fiction—if there are any trouble escalations that seem obvious, interesting, or fun to you, choose those. Every ongoing trouble has suggested escalations under its description. 

If you are unsure, roll a random result on the following table and then choose a trouble it applies to. 

## **Escalations** 

- **`1`** The trouble draws the attention of the law: take **1 Heat** . 

- **`2`** The crew lose face: forfeit **1 Rep** . 

- **`3`** Reduce a **faction status** by 1. 

- **`4`** Tick a relevant **faction clock** . 

- **`5`** Increase the **magnitude** of the ongoing trouble. 

- **`6`** Choose an **escalation** from the trouble description. 

PCs might be able to stop escalations if they’re in the right place at the right time. It’s up to you whether you offer this possibility. 

202 

## **Introducing the Trouble** 

After completing the Trouble Engine, you’ll have a few notes on faction updates, new trouble, and escalations. Introduce these into the game as you see fit (typically during the personal business phase, in and around freeplay and downtime activities). 

- Trouble might happen directly to the PCs. 

- It might be conveyed in a conversation with an NPC. 

- You might cut away from the PCs entirely to show a brief vignette of other factions or NPCs. 

- Or you might just tell the players in plain terms. 

203 

## Troubles in Detail 

## **Ongoing Troubles** 

Many troubles are momentary events, but others remain as **ongoing troubles** . They can be resolved in a variety of ways; by a score, a long-term project or some other downtime activity, or through free play. 

It’s up to you whether to set the scene and play out the trouble in full, or keep the details abstract to focus on other parts of the game. 

Sometimes ongoing troubles will resolve themselves. If it feels like a trouble has been rendered obsolete, simply remove it from the table. Maybe a faction behind the trouble has achieved what it wanted, or is distracted elsewhere, or city events have simply run their course. 

## **Light Crew Troubles** 

## **Asking Around** 

_An investigator from another faction asks around about your crew, looking for insights. They could be an Inspector, a Bluecoat, a hound, a Reality Warden, or a corporate fixer._ 

Make a **1d fortune roll** to see how much dirt they come up with in the local community (1-3: +2 Heat and the faction learns a vulnerability or secret of the crew, 4/5: +2 Heat, 6: nothing). 

## **Debt ONGOING** 

_Something has put a character or one of their close friends in the hole, and they need to make good._ 

Create a 4-segment debt clock. Fill segments by spending stacks during downtime. Until the debt clock is filled, the debt hangs over the crew and might cause various other problems. The GM might create a second clock for the creditor’s patience, escalating each time it fills. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** The debt gets bigger: increase the clock size. 

- **→** An unpleasant message is sent to the debtor. 

204 

## **Friendly Favor** 

_A friendly faction (or a contact) puts pressure on the crew to do something dangerous for them._ 

This request will usually require a full score, but could be a smaller favor. Handle it like a personal mission with a failure clock, but it’s for the whole crew. Failure means -1 faction status. 

## **Gang Trouble** 

_One of your gangs or other cohorts causes trouble due to their flaw(s)._ 

You can lose face (forfeit **Rep** equal to your Tier +1), make an example of one of the gang members, or face reprisals from the wronged party. 

## **Interrogation** 

_One of the PCs is collared and hauled in for questioning. It’s quite likely the Bluecoats, but could be any faction who have the motive and means. How did they manage to capture you?_ 

Either pay them off with **3 stacks** , or they beat you up ( **level 2 harm** ) and you tell them what they want to know ( **+3 Heat** ). You can **resist** each of those consequences separately. 

## **Patrols ONGOING** 

_A faction, probably the Bluecoats but maybe not, starts frequent patrols in your local district. They are armed and ready for trouble. They will suppress everything objectionable they can get away with._ 

Take **-1d** to the engagement roll for scores in this district. An armed patrol unit is a potential complication in any such score. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Local Unrest, Under Investigation_ 

- **→** A patrol does a **raid** on the crew’s base. 

205 

## **Rattling the Cage** 

_An unfriendly faction sends a group into the district to cause trouble. They might be testing boundaries, gathering intel, delivering payback, or just trying to provoke trouble._ 

Either leave them to it and forfeit **2 Rep per Tier of the faction** , or confront them.[This would involve a free play action roll or two—] potentially costing Stress, harm, or other consequences like negative faction status. 

## **Shakedown** 

_Someone on the periphery of your crew is forced to spill what they know about you. They will not enjoy the experience._ 

Decide which faction is behind the shakedown (Bluecoats are a good default) and identify an NPC member of the crew, a friend, or other close contact most likely to be singled out by them. Who is most vulnerable? 

Pay the culprits off with **2 stacks** , or make a fortune roll to see how much the victim talks ( **1-3: +2 Heat** ; **4/5: +1 Heat** ; **6: nothing** ; **critical:** the NPC escapes with some useful information about the faction behind the shakedown). 

Roll 2d for a normal person to see how well they keep quiet. If they’re an experienced underworld type or some kind of tough, give them 3d or 4d instead. If they’re soft or if they have some loyalty to the law, give them 1d or 0d. 

## **Thieving Bastards** 

_Thieves target the crew’s base for a heist._ 

Take away something belonging to the crew. It could be a piece of special equipment, their stacks (if they don’t have a vault), or something used as part of their downtime activities. 

This could be staged as a raid, giving the opportunity for the crew to defend themselves. 

206 

## **Things Get Weird** 

_A Class VI Metadimensional Entity (a “demon”) approaches the crew with a dark offer._ 

Their desires can be impenetrable, but the rewards can be great: money, crew upgrades, or a favor owed by this all-powerful entity. What is the request and reward? Is it made to one person, or the whole crew? 

The request might require a score, or something simpler. If the deal is completed, the reward will be delivered. If the deal is refused, a rival faction might complete it instead. 

Accept their bargain, hide until it loses interest (forfeit **3 Rep** ), or deal with it another way. 

## **Under Investigation** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Another faction has your crew under surveillance. This is probably the Bluecoats, but could be any faction with the means and motive._ 

As long as you’re under investigation, every score generates **+1 Heat** , and you take **-1d** to the engagement roll. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Interrogation, Shakedown_ 

- **→** Tipped off: This surveillance crew tips off the target of your next score. Take an additional -1d to the engagement roll (-2d total). 

## **Wrong Place, Wrong Time** 

_A friend or contact of the characters is found badly beaten. By who? Why?_ 

Let it go and lose **Rep** equal to your Tier, or go get payback. This would involve a free play action roll or two—potentially costing Stress, harm, or other consequences like negative faction status. 

207 

## **Light Local Troubles** 

## **Denounced** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Loud voices in the local district rail against the PC crew and their dangerous ways._ 

This trouble has no immediate mechanical effect, but will sour the crew’s relationship with the local citizens if it’s left unresolved. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Protests_ (against the crew) 

- **→** Take **-1 faction status** with local citizenry. 

- **→** The group behind the campaign organizes a vigilante **raid** . 

## **Disappearance** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Someone goes missing._ 

Choose a contact or friend. They disappear without immediate explanation. They could have been abducted, run away, died without anyone knowing it, been arrested... This trouble remains ongoing until their fate is known, or people stop wondering. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Sharks in the Water_ 

- **→** A ransom note appears. 

## **Disaster** 

_A storm, a fire, a flood, an anomaly tear, a hole opening up in the street... Local people are in danger._ 

Disasters are usually random city trouble, but might be deliberately caused by a faction. This might not have a direct impact on the PCs, but will they help out anyway? 

208 

## **Local Unrest** 

_The mood in the district worsens noticeably. Tempers fray, and violence is not far from the surface. Why are people angry?_ 

Actions involving angry people are always at least **risky** . 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Gang Trouble, Protests, Vandalism_ 

- **→** The crew lose face: Forfeit **1 Rep** . 

## **Moral Outrage** 

## **ONGOING** 

_A contact or a friend does something controversial, creating a local backlash against them. Is it a lifestyle choice, a reality crime, a form of protest, or something else?_ 

It’s possible some parts of the community support them, but unless the PCs figure out some kind of solution, their contact or friend may face harm (or even death). 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Local Unrest, Protests_ 

- **→** A mob tries to punish the troublemaker themselves. 

## **No-Go Zone** 

## **ONGOING** 

_A portion of the city is divided by a barrier, either between two districts or through the middle of one. It could be police barricades, a military blockade, an armed protest or insurrection, a construction project, a quarantine, or even just a mysterious wall._ 

As long as the no-go zone is in place, it’s likely to give **-1d** to **engagement rolls** for scores outside the local district, given the difficulty going to and from your base. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Local Unrest, Rioting, Vandalism_ 

**→** The zone is expanded and extended 

209 

## **Paranormal Activity** 

## **ONGOING** 

_One or more resonance entities are haunting the local district, or even the crew’s base._ 

This has no immediate effect on the crew, but might escalate into a larger problem. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Disappearance, Local Unrest, Ominous Object_ 

- **→** The activity intensifies; increase the magnitude of the trouble. 

## **Patrols** 

## **ONGOING** 

_A faction, probably the Bluecoats but maybe not, starts frequent patrols in your local district. They are armed and ready for trouble. They will suppress everything objectionable they can get away with._ 

Take **-1d** to the engagement roll for scores in this district. An armed patrol unit is a potential complication in any such score. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Local Unrest, Under Investigation_ 

- **→** A patrol does a **raid** on the crew’s base. 

## **Protests ONGOING** 

_A section of the community begins to protest some larger problem. If they call upon the crew to join them, what will the characters do?_ 

The protests will continue until the source problem is resolved, or until they run out of steam. Which section of the community is protesting? How do other groups feel about it? 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Rioting_ 

- **→** Counterprotests begin. 

210 

## **Public Outrage** 

## **ONGOING** 

_A contact or a friend does something controversial, creating a local backlash against them. Is it a lifestyle choice, a reality crime, a form of protest, or something else?_ 

Unless the PCs figure out some kind of solution, their contact or friend may face harm (or even death). 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Local Unrest, Protests_ 

- **→** A mob tries to punish the troublemaker themselves. 

## **Resource Crisis** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Something the district needs (food, medicine, plasmoleum) is suddenly inaccessible._ 

The crisis could be engineered by a rival faction, by a disastrous oversight, or as a consequence of some other effect elsewhere. A long-term project could solve it, but the right score might do so sooner. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Affliction, Local Unrest_ 

- **→** A rival faction **raids** the crew’s remaining supply of the resource. 

## **Vandalism** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Windows get broken, walls get defaced, fires are started. Is it just kids acting out, or something bigger? Is the damage random, or does it have a specific target?_ 

If it affects a friend or contact, they cannot help on downtime actions unless given a **stack** to help with repairs. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Local Unrest, Patrols, Moral Outrage_ 

- **→** Locals catch a vandal and prepare an excessive punishment. 

211 

## **Heavy Crew Troubles** 

## **Arrest** 

_The Bluecoats send a detail to arrest you._ 

**Raid.** The scale of the arrest detail is equal to your Wanted Level, at a minimum. Pay them off with **stacks** equal to your **Wanted Level +4** , hand someone over for arrest (see page 219), or defend against the raid. 

## **Assassination** 

_An enemy faction makes a lethal move against your crew._ 

**Raid** , but not necessarily on the crew’s base. What faction ordered the hit? The target might be one of the PCs or their cohorts. Alternatively, it could be a friend to a PC, or otherwise in their close circle. A good engagement roll result might mean the PCs get tipped off to the assassination in advance; a poor engagement roll might leave them desperately trying to staunch the bleeding before the target dies. 

## **Challenged Claim** 

_Another faction makes a move against one of the crew’s claims._ 

**Raid** , but on a claim. Choose a claim. The faction will make a swift and serious attack against it. If the attack is successful, the crew loses the claim and it falls under the control of the rivals. 

## **Flipped** 

_One of the PCs’ rivals arranges for one of your contacts or vice purveyors to switch allegiances due to the Heat on you. They’re loyal to another faction now._ 

The PCs lose access to the NPC in question and any associated services. Acquiring a new vice purveyor involves a long-term project. 

212 

## **Invasion** 

_An outside faction invades the district with a specific objective in mind._ 

**Raid.** The force could be a gang from another district, an elite corporate team, or a Bluecoat taskforce. They want to destroy something or grab someone. Target a person or place that matters to the crew (but probably not their base) and hit them hard. 

## **Show of Force** 

_An enemy faction makes a play against you._ 

**Raid** by a faction with whom you have a negative status. Offer them a claim to bribe them off, or defend against the raid. 

## **Heavy Local Troubles** 

Heavy local troubles never happen randomly as new trouble, but they can be a result of escalating light trouble. You can also always pick these as new trouble, either local or crew, if it makes sense to you. 

## **Affliction** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Sickness, starvation, or paranormal effects plague the district._ 

Acquire and distribute medicine, food, or some other salve to slow or halt the affliction. It may otherwise escalate. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Resource Crisis, Local Unrest, Riots_ 

- **→** The affliction spreads to other districts. 

- **→** A random PC is afflicted and takes level 2 harm (can be resisted). 

213 

## **Ominous Object** 

## **ONGOING** 

_Something strange is discovered in the local district. Maybe it’s a monolith of black stone, a large rock that fell from the sky, an unusual beast, or a gleaming silver ship that won’t open. Whatever it is, it’s a source of paranormal danger and harm._ 

Decide the manner of harm it might deliver (radiation, attracting resonance entities, sinking the district into the water, psychic trauma, etc.). Escalations will reflect this harm. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Things Get Weird, Local Unrest, Disaster, Affliction_ 

- **→** Harm to locals or to the crew. 

- **→** A faction starts making use of the object’s dark power, increasing in Tier immediately. 

## **Rioting ONGOING** 

_The streets are engulfed in waves of riots. The riots may have spread from elsewhere in the city, or been provoked by local events. The riots could have noble motives or be driven by the worst prejudices. The crew might agree with the cause or despise it._ 

Take **-1d** to the **engagement roll** , as well as **+2 Heat** , for any scores in this district while the riots are ongoing. 

## **Escalations** 

- **→** More trouble: _Vandalism, No-Go Zone, Shakedown, Patrols_ 

- **→** Rioters break into the crew’s base ( **Raid** ). 

214 

215 

## Gaining a Wanted Level 

hen the crew’s Heat level reaches 9, you gain a **Wanted Level** and clear your Heat. The higher your Wanted Level, the more serious the W response when law enforcement takes action against you (they’ll send a force of higher Quality and scale). 

Your Wanted Level also contributes to the severity of the trouble that your crew faces during each personal business phase. 

Whenever the crew gains a Wanted Level, it creates an automatic **trouble** (in addition to any trouble generated by the Trouble Engine). 

_The GM should always feel free to skip this automatic trouble if it feels like a nuisance, or doesn’t suit the current pacing of the campaign._ 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0226-06.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Wanted Level 1 Interrogation<br>Arrest:  At least 10 Bluecoats raid the base to make<br>Wanted Level 2<br>an arrest.<br>Arrest:  At least 20 Bluecoats, including a hull, raid<br>Wanted Level 3<br>the base to make an arrest.<br>Arrest:  At least 40 Bluecoats, and multiple hulls, raid<br>Wanted Level 4<br>the base to make an arrest.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Interrogation** 

_One of the PCs is collared and hauled in for questioning. It’s quite likely the Bluecoats, but could be any faction who have the motive and means. How did they manage to capture you?_ 

Either pay them off with **3 stacks** , or they beat you up ( **level 2 harm** ) and you tell them what they want to know ( **+3 Heat** ). You can **resist** each of those consequences separately. 

216 

## **Arrest** 

_The Bluecoats send a detail to arrest you._ 

**Raid.** The scale of the arrest detail is equal to your Wanted Level, at a minimum. Pay them off with **stacks** equal to your **Wanted Level +4** , hand someone over for arrest (see **Taking the Rap** in the next section), or defend against the raid. 

## **Taking the Rap** 

The only way to reduce your crew’s Wanted Level is by someone taking the rap and going to prison. When one of your PCs, a cohort, or a framed scapegoat is convicted and incarcerated for crimes associated with your crew, clear all Heat and your Wanted Level drops. 

- If you **frame a scapegoat** , the Wanted Level drops by **one** . 

- If a **cohort** takes the rap, the Wanted Level drops by **two** . 

- If a **player character** takes the rap, the Wanted Level drops by **two** . See the next section for more information about what happens to PCs who go to prison. 

_Players almost always strongly resist the idea of going to prison. The GM should act as facilitator here; never force the players to do something they don’t want to do, but you might point out that going to prison is an interesting narrative beat, and that this game has some fun prison procedures to try out, and that it’s the best way for the crew to clear Wanted Levels._ 

217 

## Prison 

## I 

ncarceration may result from investigation and arrest by the Bluecoats, or because someone turns themself in and takes the fall for the crew’s crimes. 

The severity of the prison sentence depends on your **Wanted Level** (at time of arrest, not after the level drops): 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0228-05.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Wanted Level 0 A few weeks (one score)<br>Wanted Level 1 A month or two (2–3 scores)<br>Wanted Level 2 A few months (4+ scores)<br>Wanted Level 3 A year (perhaps a whole season of your campaign)<br>Wanted Level 4 Life, execution, or Lusk Reform Technique<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


The way this is handled depends on whether the person who took the rap was a framed scapegoat, a cohort, or one of the PCs. 

## **A Note on Safety** 

Incarceration, and the carceral state more broadly, are horrible and brutal realities of our own world. Check in with Lines and Veils and other safety tools before bringing these mechanics to your table. 

218 

## **Framed Scapegoat** 

By framing a scapegoat for your crimes, you can drop your Wanted Level while continuing business as normal. You might even be able to put an enemy behind bars. Framing a scapegoat might mean the crew takes **-1 faction status** with their affiliated faction (if any), and one of the PCs should add that character as a **new rival** . 

Every few scores, or whenever they feel like it, the GM should make a fortune roll using the scapegoat’s Tier. 

- **1-3** They do their time and keep their head down, focused on getting through this. 

- **4/5** They nurse their grudge against the crew. Make a 10-clock for their vengeance and tick it twice. Tick it again every time they get this result. 

- **6** They work toward getting out, legally or illegally. Add +1 to this roll next time. If their vengeance is at 4 or more, they arrange for someone to deal payback against the crew on the outside (appropriate to the scapegoat’s Tier and faction affiliations). 

- **Crit** They either successfully appeal their wrongful conviction, or else escape Ironhook by jailbreak. Either way, they’re back on the streets. 

## **Cohort** 

If a cohort takes the rap, the Wanted Level drops by **two** . Whether the cohort is a gang or an expert, you lose access to them while they serve their sentence (because gang members are small fry, the entire gang has to take the fall). 

When the cohort’s sentence is complete, make a fortune roll using their Quality. Remember that when a cohort’s type applies, you can use their full Quality rating, but otherwise the Quality is 0—decide together whether you think the cohort’s type will help them in prison. 

- **1-3** They lose their nerve and flip on the crew. Take **+4 Heat** , and remove the cohort from the crew sheet. They get released and go into hiding. 

- **4/5** They keep their head down and get through it. They gain the flaw _Demoralized_ (or they might instead lose an appropriate edge, such as _Loyal_ or _Tenacious_ ). 

- **6** They do their time well. The crew gains **+1 faction status** with a faction that they assisted while inside. 

- **Crit** They make a name for the crew inside. Gain **+2 Rep** for the crew, and **1 prison claim** . 

219 

## **Player Character** 

While your _character_ almost certainly doesn’t want to go to prison, as a player this can be a surprisingly useful and interesting choice. It’s natural, as a player, that you don’t want to give up, don’t want to give the Bluecoats what they want, don’t want to _lose_ , but consider this as a narrative opportunity. It can be an interesting part of your character’s unfolding story, and offers several potential benefits to the rest of the crew. 

_Hulls don’t go to Ironhook, and if a Hull takes the fall for the crew’s crimes, they’ll either be decommissioned or have their memory wiped._ 

If your PC goes to Ironhook, you have a few options: 

- You can follow it in **real time** , playing a different character while your incarcerated PC serves their sentence. You’ll check in with them every personal business phase, and when they get out, you can start playing them again. 

- Or, the whole table can agree to do a **time skip** , jumping ahead months or even years to when your character is released. This way, your character doesn’t miss any of the action on-screen. 

- Alternatively, you could decide that this is character is no longer a part of the story (especially if they’re serving a life sentence or similar). Start playing a new character, and perhaps you check in with the incarcerated character in an epilogue at the end of the campaign. 

220 

## **PC in Prison: Real Time** 

You play a different character while the campaign continues as normal, and the character in prison misses a number of scores roughly equal to the sentence length (time-keeping is intentionally vague, both in terms of the sentence and how much time elapses between scores, so skip a number of scores that feels narratively appropriate but still fun for everyone at the table). 

You’ll check in with your incarcerated character every personal business phase, so they remain a part of the narrative. They make rolls and choose prison activities, which can help the crew from inside. 

While your PC is in prison, you could play: 

- **A friend, contact, or another NPC** and have them join the crew. When you make a new character because one has gone to prison, they start with bonus special abilities equal to your crew Tier, and also bonus action dots equal to your crew Tier. 

- **An entirely new character.** 

- **A paranormal character.** This can be an opportunity to try something weird and different for a while. The Time Traveler is a great choice since they’re designed to stick around for a temporary number of scores before being flung back to their own time, but Ghost/Echo or Vampire are fun choices too. 

## **Prison Activities** 

Every personal business phase, a PC in prison gets two **prison activities** . The first activity must always be **survival** (see the next page). The second can be chosen from the rest of the list. 

You always get two prison activities, even when the crew is at war. 

You can buy more prison activities for the cost of 1 stack per additional activity, but by default you’re sent to prison with no items and no money (when you start your prison sentence, move any stacks into the bank or give them to a friend to hold on to—either way, clear them off your sheet). 

If you want money or other items, it will need to be smuggled in by someone on the outside (requiring an action roll, with potential consequences like Heat, harm, losing visitor access, etc.), or by doing a favor for a faction inside. 

221 

## **Survival** 

The GM describes a challenge facing the PC in prison this session. It could be a rival gang, the cruelty of the guards or warden, prison labor, paranormal activity, or anything else. These challenges might be connected between sessions, or they might be unique. 

The player describes how they meet the challenge and makes an appropriate action roll, with position and effect as usual (risky/standard by default). 

On a **1-3** or **4/5** , they take consequences appropriate to the situation and their position. This could include harm, Heat, negative faction status, and/or slipping into a desperate position for the next survival check. 

On a **6** , they deal with the situation and avoid any negative consequences. On a critical, they turn the situation to their advantage and clear 3 Stress. 

Consequences can be resisted, but note that prisoners cannot indulge their vice to clear Stress, and so any Stress spent will quickly accumulate into deadlocks. 

After the survival roll, **put one tick on the healing clock** . This is the only way to recover harm in prison—slowly. Harm will rapidly accumulate—it’s your choice whether to erode your body or your spirit, but either way you will be worn down. This is the prison system working as intended. 

**Wanted Level 4:** If your PC got a life sentence, a 1-3 result on a desperate survival check might indicate their fate takes a drastic turn for the worse. They may be sentenced to execution, or to the Lusk Reform Technique (their echo stripped out and put into a hull). Can they break out of prison before this happens? 

222 

## **Other Prison Activities** 

Every criminal faction in Doskvol has a connection to Ironhook, whether through incarcerated crew members, friends, family, or trusted associates. There are deals to be made here, levers to be pulled—a smart crew can gain a lot with a person on the inside. 

**Do a Favor:** The GM will tell you who needs a favor and what it is. Decide how you go about it and roll one of your actions. **1-3:** Earn 1 stack or mark 1 crew Rep, but also take a minor consequence (level 1 harm, 1 Heat, etc.), **4/5:** Earn 1 stack or mark 1 crew Rep, **6:** Earn 1 stack and mark 1 crew Rep, **critical:** Earn 2 stacks and mark 2 crew Rep. 

**Make Friends:** Improving faction status from within prison is a **4-segment prison clock** . Choose a faction (probably an underworld one, but other factions are possible), describe what your character does to advance the clock, and roll one of your actions. **1-3:** one segment, **4/5:** two segments, **6:** three segments, **critical:** five segments (rolls over into a new clock). When the clock is filled, the crew takes **+1 status** with that faction. Also, if the current faction status is positive, add a new friend to your character sheet (a member of that faction inside prison with you). 

**Gather Intel:** Describe what your character does to keep an ear to the ground and sniff out useful information, and roll one of your actions. This determines the quality of intel you find out, but you don’t have to decide what it relates to—note down that you have it, and the details can be decided later in a flashback (e.g. during the next score). **1-3:** Incomplete or partial information, **4/5:** Good details, with a follow-up question allowed, **6:** Exceptional details, with multiple follow-up questions, **critical:** Same as 6, and also clear 2 Stress. 

**Unlock a Prison Claim:** These are special claims specific to prison that will make doing your time easier (and make it easier for other crew members who do time in the future). Taking a prison claim is a **6-segment clock** . Describe what your character does to advance the project clock, and roll one of your actions. **1-3:** one segment, **4/5:** two segments, **6:** three segments, **critical:** five segments. When the clock is filled, choose a new claim connected to one of your existing claims. 

**Long-Term Project:** You can work on a standard long-term project to achieve any goals not covered by the preceding activities. The GM will tell you the size of the prison clock; it may be limited by the tools or resources you have available inside. Describe what your character does to advance the project clock, and roll one of your actions. **1-3:** one segment, **4/5:** two segments, **6:** three segments, **critical** : five segments. 

**Discover an Opportunity:** During the next planning meeting, this character can present their own **opportunity** , representing a lead they sniffed out from inside Ironhook. When rolling for payout, roll a dice pool equal to crew **Tier +2** . 

223 

## **Real Time Prison XP** 

PCs in prison mark xp for desperate actions as normal, and can hit xp triggers at the end of any session where they played a part. 

## **PC in Prison: Time Skip** 

By taking a time skip, your character won’t miss a score (on screen, at least). This lets you explore the narrative of one of the PCs going to prison (and the potential benefits) without that character missing out on any actual gameplay. 

Follow the standard **time skip** procedure for the rest of the crew. 

The incarcerated PC instead makes a **Doing Time roll** . Assemble a dice pool: 

- Start with your **worst attribute rating** (Insight, Prowess, or Resolve). Incarceration is dehumanizing and brutal and will test you at every level, probing for weak points. 

- Add your **crew’s Tier** . The renown of your crew is your best defense inside. 

- Add +1d for any advantages, such as allies inside, and take -1d for any disadvantages, such as influential enemies inside. 

Roll the dice pool and assign **one die** to each of these outcomes: **physical toll** , **mental toll** , and **schemes** . Results cannot be resisted. 

224 

## **Mental Toll** 

**Deadlock one key** , and you emerge with **4 Stress** marked (4 total, **1-3** not an extra 4). 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0235-03.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
4/5 Mark an extra  4 Stress  (which might deadlock a key).<br>You worked through some of your issues.  Turn one deadlock back<br>6<br>into a key.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Physical Toll** 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0235-05.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
You come out of prison with  level 2 harm .<br>1-3 If you went to prison at Wanted Level 4, you don’t come out of<br>prison, and you’re scheduled for either imminent execution or the<br>Lusk Reform Technique (your echo stripped out and put into a hull).<br>4/5 You come out with  level 1 harm .<br>You come out stronger.  Add one dot  to any action under the Prowess<br>6<br>attribute.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Schemes** 

Your schemes blow back on you. Your crew takes **-1 status** with **1-3** one faction. Your crew gains **1 prison claim** and **+1 faction status** with a faction **4/5** that you assisted while you served your time. You gain **+3 Rep** for your crew, **1 prison claim** , and **+1 faction status 6** with a faction that you assisted while you served your time. 

225 

## **Prison Claims** 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0236-02.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
SMUGGLING CONTRABAND CELL BLOCK CONTROL<br>+2 load in prison & Can now Indulge  No more<br>can carry stacks, Vice inside prison, survival rolls, +2d<br>+1d to Doing Time +1d to Doing Time to Doing Time rolls<br>HARDCASE OUTSIDE CLAIM<br>Mark turf box<br>PR ISON Take a claim<br>on crew’s<br>for the crew<br>rep tracker<br>GUARD PAYOFF SMUGGLING PAROLE INFLUENCE<br>+1d to all rolls +2 load in prison & Wanted level<br>inside prison, can carry stacks, counts as one<br>+1d to Doing Time +1d to Doing Time lower for sentence<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**Cell Block Control:** Your crew has a cell block under their total control— guards and all. Survival rolls are no longer a mandatory prison activity when doing time. When cohorts do time, increase the result level of their prison roll by one (a 1-3 becomes a 4/5, etc.). If you choose a time skip and make a Doing Time prison roll, take +2d. 

**Contraband:** Your crew has solid black market contacts. Indulge Vice is unlocked as a prison activity. If you choose a time skip and make a Doing Time prison roll, take +1d. 

**Guard Payoff:** You claim several prison guards on your payroll. Take +1d to all rolls inside prison (for both PCs and cohorts). If you choose a time skip and make a Doing Time prison roll, take +1d. 

**Hardcase:** Your reputation as a tough inmate bolsters your crew’s image. This counts as turf; mark one of the turf boxes on your crew’s Rep tracker (it takes one less Rep to advance). 

**Outside Claim:** One of your allies on the inside arranges for their faction to help your crew out. Take a claim for your crew. You can’t take turf or one-off opportunities with this claim. 

226 

**Parole Influence:** Political pressures of various sorts can be applied to the magistrates and warden who oversee sentences for crimes. With this claim, you’re always able to arrange for a shorter prison stay—as if your Wanted Level was 1 lower. So, if your Wanted Level was 3 when you went in, you’d spend only several months behind bars (equivalent to level 2) instead of a full year. 

**Smuggling:** You arrange smuggling channels inside. You have +2 load while incarcerated, (starting from zero as a prisoner), and can enter prison with stacks, or transfer stacks from your bank. If you take this claim twice, you’ll have 4 load while you’re serving time. You may reset the items in your prison loadout each personal business phase. If you choose a time skip and make a Doing Time prison roll, take +1d. 

227 

## Time Skips 

## S 

ometimes it’s worth skipping ahead in time with a sharp cut, jumping forward by weeks, months, or even years. There are several benefits to a time skip: 

- **It can make things feel more believable.** There might be narrative beats that involve internal character growth, or external changes like an expanding criminal empire, that feel more natural and believable if they occur off-screen during a time skip, rather than squeezing it all into the week or two between scores. A time skip is a golden opportunity to make big decisions and dramatic changes. 

- **It prunes the branches.** The nature of the game is that the PC crew bounces around the underworld like a pinball, triggering reactions and complications and trouble. This generates a lot of drama and excitement, but sometimes it feels like there are too many narrative branches, too many balls in the air, a little too much going on. A time skip lets you sweep the board clean and focus on the most interesting narrative threads. 

- **It’s exciting.** The endless cycle of crew business and personal business can sometimes feel rhythmic and predictable. A time skip jolts the senses and wakes everyone up. It prompts you to lean forward and pay attention. 

A time skip should only happen by agreement of everyone at the table. You can have a time skip at any time, but here are some good opportunities: 

- When the crew **advances Tier** . 

- If a PC **goes to prison** . 

- **At the end of a season** of your campaign, e.g. after the crew completes a big one-off opportunity. 

- **If you’ve had a real-world break** from the game for a while, and you can’t quite remember everything that was going on. 

By definition, time that is skipped is time that is not worth showing on screen. Sometimes something isn’t shown on screen because it’s more interesting to present it _fait accompli_ , and have it explained after the fact (or not explained at all). But mostly it’s because this time period is business as usual and although the status quo will change, it won’t change much. 

Once the time skip has been agreed, each player makes a **time passes** roll for their PC. Then someone makes one for the crew as a whole. Finally, the GM updates trouble and faction clocks. 

Go straight into a planning meeting or a cold open. After the score begins, each player can reintroduce their character as they take action. 

228 

## **Time Passes Roll (PC)** 

This is a fortune roll, starting with 1d. Modify the dice pool for any major advantages or disadvantages that apply. 

## **Advantages** 

- For each **friend** , take +1d. 

- If you have a **backing faction** , take +1d. 

- For each **full row of bank** , take +1d. 

- For each **long-term project** you have underway, take +1d. 

- Are there any **other elements** that you want to consider? Perhaps you have a special ability that seems relevant, such as the Swinger’s ability _Always Hustling_ . Take +1d for each relevant advantage. 

## **Disadvantages** 

- For each **rival** , take -1d. 

- If your crew is **at war** , take -1d. 

- For each **deadlock** , take -1d. 

- Are there any **other elements** that you want to consider? Perhaps you personally insulted a powerful resonance entity who has vowed revenge. Take -1d for each relevant disadvantage. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0239-14.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1-3 Chose  one win  and  two setbacks .<br>4/5 Choose  one win  and  one setback .<br>6 Choose  two wins  and  one setback .<br>Critical Choose  two wins .<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


229 

After the roll, **clear your harm** and **reset your Stress to zero** . Select wins and setbacks from the following lists. Then consider your look and what you’re wearing—change or add something to show the passage of time (or change it all up). 

## **Wins** 

- **Add a new friend.** This could be a person, animal, or even a resonance entity. Choose from your playbook sheet or make up a new one. 

- **Complete a long-term project.** Choose one of your long-term projects (or decide a new one) and complete it immediately. 

- **Make bank.** Add +6 to your bank. 

- **Gain a backing faction.** Choose any faction of any Tier. You’ll still need to complete a personal mission to unlock their prestige ability, as usual. 

- **Turn a deadlock back into a key.** How do you work through the deadlock? Does it turn back into its original key, or something new? 

- **Add a new playbook item** chosen from a different playbook. How did your character end up with this personal item? 

## **Setbacks** 

- **Add a new rival.** Choose from your playbook sheet or make up a new one. The GM might also have ideas on who it could be, based on the factions and NPCs you’ve antagonized so far. Can only be selected once. 

- **Take harm.** Mark one passing harm (level 1) and one major harm (level 2). The original harm was much worse, but it has partially healed. Did you suffer the harm as part of crew dealings, or in your personal affairs? Choose a visual signifier of this harm, either permanent (an eyepatch, a scar) or temporary (bandages, crutches). 

- **Deadlock a key.** Choose any key and deadlock it immediately. 

- **Indebted.** Add the ongoing trouble _Debt_ . Create a 4-segment debt clock. Fill segments by spending stacks during downtime. Until the debt clock is filled, the debt hangs over the PC and might cause various other problems. 

- **A long-term project fails.** Choose a project clock with at least two ticks completed. It fails. 

Eventually, it will be good to know the details of these wins and setbacks. You don’t need to answer that immediately, though, and in fact it’s easier and more fun to introduce the change first (such as a pet albino snake, or a broken arm) and only reveal how and why you got it later. This also saves you from having to think of everything all at once. 

230 

## **Time Passes Roll (Crew)** 

This is a fortune roll, starting with dice equal to the **crew’s Tier** . Modify the dice pool for any major advantages or disadvantages that apply. 

## **Advantages** 

- For each **allied faction** (+3 status), take +1d. 

- For each PC that has a **backing faction** , take +1d. 

- Are there any **other elements** that you want to consider? Take +1d for each relevant advantage. 

## **Disadvantages** 

- If the crew is **at war** , take -2d. 

- If the crew has **weak hold** , take -1d. 

- For each **ongoing trouble** , take -1d. 

- Are there any **other elements** that you want to consider? Take -1d for each relevant disadvantage. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0241-12.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
1-3 Chose  one win  and  two setbacks .<br>4/5 Choose  one win  and  one setback .<br>6 Choose  two wins  and  one setback .<br>Critical Choose  two wins .<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


After the roll, **reset the crew’s Heat to zero** . Select wins and setbacks from the lists on the next page. 

231 

## **Wins** 

- Crew puts **+3 stacks** in the coffers. 

- One cohort gains a new **edge** , or loses a **flaw** . 

- Mark **4 crew xp** . 

- Choose a new piece of **base flair** and gain **one crew upgrade** . 

- **+1 faction status** with any faction of the players’ choice. This can include making allies, and it can end an ongoing war. 

- **Push the ticking timebomb back three ticks.** 

## **Setbacks** 

- **Crew marks +5 Heat.** 

- **-1 faction status** with any faction of the GM’s choice. The faction must currently be anywhere from -1 status up to +2 status; this cannot put you at war, and it cannot break an alliance. Does an existing relationship degrade, or are there new competitors on the scene? 

- **A PC or cohort gets arrested.** They’re currently being held in the local precinct station. Will you attempt to bust them out, or let them take the fall? 

- **The ticking timebomb advances two ticks.** 

- **The GM advances a clock of their choice by four ticks** (but not the ticking timebomb). 

## **Tidy Up Clocks and Trouble** 

The GM considers ongoing troubles, the ticking timebomb, and any clocks on the table. There’s no procedure to this, so just go with your gut instinct. This is a narrative choice—don’t worry about what would realistically happen. Keep stuff that feels interesting, remove anything that’s not. This is a golden opportunity to pare back and simplify. If in doubt, ask the players, or make a fortune roll and let the dice decide. 

If you’ve decided on a time skip in the middle of a session, feel free to park this part until you have time to get to it (e.g. between sessions). 

- **Ongoing troubles.** Remove any troubles that feel stale, irrelevant, or otherwise fail to spark joy in you as GM. 

- **Ticking timebomb.** Add a tick, or not. You can increase the sense of the situation advancing over time (lots of posters have gone up, more protests, etc.) without actually advancing the clock. Alternatively, if you want to suddenly make this a central concern, add as many clock ticks as you want. Just be careful that the players don’t feel punished for choosing a time skip. 

232 

- **Other clocks.** Remove, add, and advance clocks if you want. Don’t feel you have to, though, especially for clocks the players aren’t aware of. Remember that by definition a time skip is mostly a boring period of time, and that’s why we skip it. Maybe the clocks don’t change dramatically. Again, if you want to make a particular faction clock suddenly relevant, it’s also a good excuse to tick something multiple times. Make a fortune roll if you’re not sure. 

- **Add new trouble.** You don’t need to run the Trouble Engine entirely, but consider choosing an ongoing trouble that’s arisen during the time skip. Something unsettling can be a fun choice; _Disappearance_ , _No-Go Zone_ , or _Ominous Object_ . 

## **Now Then, Where Were We?** 

After Time Passes rolls have been made, with wins and setbacks chosen, jump back into the action. Launch straight into a cold open, or do a quick planning meeting to select the first score back. Make an engagement roll and away you go. As each character takes action, their player should talk a little bit about what looks different about them and how they’ve changed over the time skip. 

As you proceed through the score and into the personal business phase, you can talk together about what occurred during the time skip, how clocks have changed, the situation in the city—but don’t try to cover it all at once. 

233 

## Raids 

## S 

ometimes personal business will end with the crew being raided, due to the Trouble Engine or the demands of the fiction. Skip the planning S meeting—this is the next “score” now (there is no payout). The GM will cut straight to the action as the raid begins. 

## **Raid Engagement Roll** 

Skip the approach and go straight to the engagement roll. Start with 1d, and then consider: 

- Are the assailants exploiting a vulnerability of the crew or their base? Take -1d. 

- Are the PCs distracted, surprised, or otherwise not at their best? Take -1d. 

- Do the PCs have special preparations or defences against this kind of approach? Take +1d. 

- Are any friends or contacts able and willing to provide warning? Take +1d. 

- Are any other enemies or rivals helping the raid succeed? Take -1d. 

- Are the assailants higher Tier than the crew? Take -1d. 

- Are the assailants lower Tier than the crew? Take +1d. 

**1-3:** Bad result. You’re in a **desperate** position when the raid starts. Each PC has a **max load of 2** . The assailants may make an ultimatum at this point, and if the PCs accept their proposal, then the raid is called off (return to the end of personal business and a planning meeting as usual). 

_The Crowfoot Chancers wake to the sound of shouting in the lounge. A squad of Bluecoats have Hadwig in cuffs. “This one’s under arrest. Let us go peacefully and you can go back to bed. Or make a fuss and you can all join her in a cell. What’ll it be?”_ 

**4/5:** Mixed result. You’re in a **risky** position, and each PC has a **max load of 3** . The raid is under way, and the crew doesn’t have much warning. 

_The Crowfoot Chancers are woken by the sound of a battering ram smashing their front door off its hinges. They have just enough time to roll out of bed and grab whatever weapon they keep to hand._ 

**6:** Good result. You’re in a **controlled** position when the action starts, and each PC has a **max load of 4** . 

234 

_The Crowfoot Chancers wake to the sound of boots in the stairwell. They have a few moments to gear up and prepare before the Bluecoats start hammering at the door._ 

**Critical:** Exceptional result. **Each PC has a max load of 6** . You’re aware of the raid before they even reach your location, and you can counter-ambush them at a place of your choosing. 

## **Resolution** 

Don’t be coy about what the assailants want; if it isn’t worth fighting against, the PCs might choose to back off and retreat. Depending on the engagement roll and the situation, they might need to make further action rolls to slip away cleanly. 

If they do choose to fight, it’s up to them to drive the assailants off. This will almost certainly result in a negative faction status with the assailants, and possibly a lot of Heat (especially for factions like the Bluecoats, or other institutions of the law). 

If the raid was played out in full, do aftermath, unwind, and then personal business as usual. 

235 

## Backing Factions 

## A 

ny PC can gain their own individual backing faction, formalizing a friendly relationship with that faction, and eventually unlocking prestige abilities. 

## **Gaining a Backing Faction** 

Gaining a faction’s backing requires completing a long-term project. The size of the clock is based on the faction Tier. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0246-06.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Tier 1 4-clock<br>Tier 2 4-clock<br>Tier 3 6-clock<br>Tier 4 6-clock<br>Tier 5 8-clock<br>Tier 6 10-clock<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


You can work on this with a Long-Term Project downtime action, as usual. You should also add ticks to the clock to reflect the fiction—if you complete a score that’s beneficial to the faction, perhaps you should tick your backing faction clock too. Discuss with the GM. Likewise, negative interactions might remove ticks. 

Each PC can only have the backing of one faction at a time. You can dump a backing faction at any time. 

236 

## **Faction Help** 

Once you have a backing faction, you can call on that faction for help—either during crew business or personal business. 

## **Types of Help** 

**Assist** on your action roll, as if they were a teammate (+1d). 

**Increase effect** on your action roll, as though you pushed yourself. 

Loan you **equipment** of their Tier level in Quality. 

Share relevant intel with you; make a **gather information** roll with dice equal to their Tier. 

Give you **+1 result level** for a downtime activity. 

Give you a **1 stack reward** when handing over information of interest to them. 

**Resist or dismiss unwanted consequences** , if it’s within their powers. 

**Intervene with another faction** on your behalf, if it’s within their powers. 

Sometimes the GM may want a fortune roll to see whether they can help, depending on the context. **Each type of help can only be called on once per cycle of crew business and personal business.** Make a note of it when you call on help. 

237 

## **Return the Favor** 

After your backing faction helps you, they might ask for something in return. At the start of personal business, make a fortune roll. Use one die for each instance of help received since the last time you made this roll. 

## **Return the Favor Results** 

**1-3** Nothing, but add **+1d** next time you make this roll. **4/5** They ask for a **minor favor** . They give you a **personal mission** . If you already have one active, **6** add one tick to the failure clock. They give you an **urgent personal mission** —mark the failure **Critical** clock twice immediately. If you already have one active, add one tick to the failure clock. 

## **Minor Favors** 

Minor favors do not usually require a downtime action, and can be completed with free play. See the following table for some inspiration. 

An action roll may be appropriate, sometimes, with possible consequences including minor harm, 1 Heat for the crew, or ticking a relevant clock (such as faction suspicion, anger, etc.). This shouldn’t spiral into multiple action rolls— minor favors should be short and simple. 

Some minor favors might be more pointed. _Deliver some minor intel on one of your crewmates or their own backing faction. Arrange for one of your crewmates to be in a particular place and time for surveillance and/or framing._ 

For paranormal and fringe backing factions, occult favors might be more wellsuited. _Draw a particular sign in chalk or salt at a particular location. Allow yourself to be possessed by an echo for an evening. Attend a séance. Share your paranormal research._ 

238 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0249-01.png)


**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Minor Favors<br>1 – 3 4—6<br>1 Share or teach some of your skills. Deliver a package.<br>Attend a social event. Convince a tabloid to run (or not run)<br>2<br>a particular story.<br>Delay a target so they miss an  Cause a distraction at a particular<br>3 important train/airship/meeting/  time and place.<br>drop-off/etc.<br>Give a faction member a place to lay  Intimidate a target, to send a<br>4<br>low for a while. message.<br>Babysitting. A particular NPC needs  Serve as an alibi for a faction<br>to be kept out of the way for a night.  member, and testify in court.<br>5<br>Take them out dancing, see a movie,<br>whatever—just keep them busy.<br>Clean a crime scene before the  Store a duffel bag in your base for<br>6<br>Bluecoats arrive. one night. Don’t look inside.<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Personal Missions** 

Personal missions usually require a score. This will probably target one of the backing faction’s enemies, and might involve taking a claim, stealing something, or simply putting that faction in their place. 

If a personal mission involves a meaningful sacrifice, a score may not be required. For example, the backing faction may ask the PC to go to Ironhook to turn or target a particular inmate or guard. Going to prison is a major sacrifice, so no score is required. 

_For the GM, personal missions are your opportunity to put a thumb on the scale and nudge events toward a narratively satisfying end. Consider what the faction would want, but also consider what will drive the season toward an exciting and dramatic climax._ 

Create a 4-segment failure clock. Tick it every downtime that the PC fails to complete the personal mission. When the clock is filled, the faction considers the mission failed and the PC loses the faction’s backing for now (another long-project can restore it). 

It’s up to that player to present an opportunity to the rest of their crew that will fulfill the personal mission, and convince their crewmates to take it on. The personal mission is complete when the backing faction is satisfied it is complete. 

239 

## **Prestige Ability** 

Once you’ve successfully completed a personal mission for your backing faction, you immediately gain the listed **prestige ability** —a special ability, item, or permission. Also, add **+1** to the crew’s **status** with that faction. 

As an example, these are the prestige abilities for Paxton and Red Torque: 

## **Prestige Ability** 

**Big Night Out:** If you **Indulge Vice** drinking with Paxton, **add 4** to the dice outcome to determine how much Stress you clear. An ally who joins in your vice may do the same. If you overindulge, pick two consequences instead of one, and the crew marks 2 Rep. 

## **Prestige Ability** 

**Afterburner:** Bring a vehicle to their secret chopshop (ahead of time or in a flashback) and they’ll outfit it with one of their custom Red Torque afterburners. Activate it once per score for a temporary burst of explosive acceleration. 

Consider whether your character needs to miss a score or two while they head off to complete some training or similar. If so, play another character in the interim, or take a time skip. 

As you continue to develop your relationship with this faction and complete more personal missions, you might earn further perks and prestige abilities. Follow the fiction. You can use the other prestige abilities listed in the Faction Rolodex as inspiration for new ones. 

## **Negative Faction Status** 

You do not have to have a positive faction status with a backing faction, either to gain them as a backing faction in the first place, or to unlock their prestige ability, or to keep the ability. 

Faction status tracks the relationship of the crew to other factions, and doesn’t necessarily reflect the personal status of each PC. 

In fact, choosing a hostile faction as a backing faction can lead to interesting opportunities. You can steer your enemies in your desired direction and gain important insight into their operations. Double agents, triple agents, cat-andmouse games; who’s playing who? 

As always, though, follow the fiction—if it feels like the only possible response is for a backing faction to kick a PC out, perhaps after a particularly egregious betrayal, then that is what happens. 

240 

## **Losing Prestige Abilities** 

Losing a backing faction may mean you lose access to the prestige ability. This depends on the fictional specifics of the ability. Training can’t be revoked, but access to reinforcements can, for example. Negotiate the exact outcome with the GM, but try to be generous in your interpretation. 

241 

## ART PLACEHOLDER 

242 

## Preparing for the First Session 

uring the first session, the group will choose a crew type, create their characters, and then get dropped into an explosive cold open. D 

To prepare, read through **character creation** (page 11) and **crew creation** (page 86). Skim through the **Faction Rolodex** (page 342) and note down a few that grab your attention. During crew creation, the players will ask about some factions connected to their crew, so it’s good to have some favorites in mind. 

_Different factions will work better for different crew types, so don’t get too locked in. Dealers, for example, might be more entangled with smugglers and rival dealers, while Utopians might be more deeply entangled with the weird and fringe factions. On the flip side, an unexpected faction relationship is a good way to spice things up and keep it interesting._ 

Have a browse through the **cold opens** (page 256) and the **ticking timebombs** (page 272). Cold opens provide a short, dynamic score to kick off your campaign, while ticking timebombs are a background situation that heightens the dramatic tension, bubbling away in the background of the crew’s activities. Like starting factions, some cold opens and ticking timebombs might suit certain crew types better than others. 

Think about what communication tools you want to use (page 9) and how you want to introduce _Blades ’68_ . You might want to discuss the kind of game it is and what sort of tone you’re going for. Are any of the players unfamiliar with _Blades in the Dark_ ? How much will you need to explain? 

Try to keep it short. You don’t need to explain mechanics until they come up in play. Focus on telling the players why you’re excited to play this game, and the parts you think they’ll enjoy. 

243 

Finally, print out game sheets if you’re playing in-person. There are various sheets available: 

## **Crew Sheet** 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0254-03.png)


Contains a one-page crew sheet for each of the six crew types. Either **print one copy of each crew sheet** and the players can choose at the table, or have them pick ahead of time and print only the page you need. 

Includes special abilities, claims, Heat, Rep, upgrades, etc. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0254-06.png)


## **Crew Base Sheet** 

A general crew sheet that supplements the specific crew type sheet. **Print one of these for the table.** 

Space to track base flair, upgrades, and cohorts, as well as important crew rules summaries. 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0254-12.png)


## **Playbook Sheets** 

Contains a one-page sheet for each of the eight playbooks, and the additional four paranormal playbooks. Either 

**print one copy of each basic playbook** and the players can choose at the table, or have them pick ahead of time and print only the playbooks you need. 

244 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0255-01.png)


## **Player Cheat Sheet** 

A general reference sheet that supplements the playbook sheets. **Print one of these for each player.** 

Includes space to track backing faction details, as well as rules summaries for action rolls, flashbacks, gather information, downtime activities, etc. 

Every player should have two sheets—one specific playbook, and a cheat sheet.

A one-page summary of the character creation process. It helps players go through character creation without the main book or the GM’s help, but it’s not strictly necessary.

Other sheets that aren’t required but might be useful include: 


![](Blades_Markdown_Reader/images/blades68_v0.9.5_textonly.pdf-0256-02.png)
