## **GM Run Sheet** 

A checklist of the various phases of play, with space to write notes. Helpful for remembering where you are in the order of operations, and what you’ve done so far. 

## **Prison Sheet** 

Space to track prison claims, as well as a summary of both main prison procedures. 

You don’t need to print this until prison becomes a possibility. 

## **The Player Gazette** 

A summary of the various factions and districts for player reference at the table. Not required, but can be very useful—especially when players are trying to pick a backing faction. 

Try stapling each section separately (underworld factions, institutional factions, districts, etc.). This keeps them digestible and lets different players reference different parts of the gazette at the same time. 

246 

## Future Sessions 

## P 

lay out the cold open, either during the first session or finishing up in the second session. This counts as a score, so after it’s complete, you move into the **aftermath** (page 190) and an **unwind scene** . 

Then your first gameplay cycle begins in earnest— **personal business** and downtime activities (page 164), followed by a **planning meeting** (page 180), and into another **score** (page 188). 

At some point, the GM will run the **Trouble Engine** (page 197), creating entanglements for the crew to deal with. 

Most groups seem to get through either personal business or a score in one session, so you could aim for that to begin with. Adjust pacing to suit your own session length and frequency, and your own tastes. 

## **Opening Titles** 

Begin each session with opening titles, a short procedure that acts as a bookend to the session (the other bookend is going over xp at the end). This is a good way to transition out of casual catch-up chat and into the game proper. 

Imagine opening titles like a (vintage) TV show—a montaged sequence of various characters, places, and moments that are important to the show. If you’ve chosen a theme song for your campaign, the opening titles are a good time to play it in the background. 

Each player take turns describing a very brief scene (a single shot). The GM does the same in between each player. 

_If you have three players and a GM, you might start with one player, then the GM, followed by the next player, then the GM, and finally the third player. The GM could go again at the end, or choose not to._ 

**Players** describe their character in a quintessential moment; a classic example of that character being that character. 

_The Swinger sliding across the hood of their car, the Radical delivering a speech on a soapbox, the Paranormalist talking to a strange entity in the mirror._ 

Once you’ve done opening titles a few times, mix it up with a creative prompt (suggested by either a player or the GM). Every player responds to the same prompt. 

247 

## **Opening title prompt ideas:** 

- A moment of action from a score we’ve never seen. 

- The everyday business of your crew. 

- Your character “suiting up” before the score. 

- A moment of quiet reflection. 

- A moment of your character helping another PC. 

- Scenes from last session. 

- A scene from later in tonight’s session (a flash-forward). 

**The GM** takes a turn in between each player and describes a moment from the wider world of Doskvol ’68. These might be little moments from the local district, a glimpse of an allied or antagonistic faction, or anything else. They’re a chance to paint in the rest of the world that we don’t usually see, the world beyond the player characters. Consider using: 

- **District** write-ups (page 387). Each contains suggested scenes, and these are great to drop into the opening titles. Start with the crew’s local district, and expand from there. 

- **Factions** (page 342). Both friendly and antagonistic. Give a hint as to what they’re up to, their project clocks, or their reactions to the activities of the PC crew. _Goons being berated for failing to stop that heist—a gleaming silver rocketship being constructed—a mysterious figure dressed all in white, looking down on Doskvol with a martini in hand._ 

- **Friends, rivals, and other contacts.** Quiet moments that paint a picture of these people’s everyday lives; their successes and their struggles. 

- **The Ticking Timebomb** (page 272). As the timebomb’s situation develops, you can show what that looks like. _A security camera being installed on the local street corner._ 

- **Trouble** (page 197). If you’ve run the Trouble Engine and you know what trouble is coming up, foreshadow it here. _Twenty Bluecoats assembling in the precinct yard before marching into vans._ 

- **The next score.** If they’ve chosen their next score and you’ve done some prep, you can foreshadow what they’ll encounter. _The security team gearing up for their shift—the caterers hanging decorations and preparing_ — 

- _platters of hors d’oeuvres a huge diamond resting on a velvet cushion._ 

248 

## **Campaign Concepts** 

_Blades ’68_ is plenty of fun as a standalone campaign, but you might also consider: 

- _**Blades in the Dark**_ **sequel.** If you’ve played one or more campaigns of core _Blades_ , consider adapting the world of ’68 to reflect the events of those campaigns. _Perhaps the Dimmer Sisters ended up in a position of power, and you want to reflect that in ’68._ What happened to the player characters of the prior campaign? What legacy have they left behind, 100 years later? 

- **Back to the past.** You might play a regular campaign of _Blades ’68_ and then jump back in time for a session (or campaign) of _Blades in the Dark_ . What events lead to the future you’ve already witnessed? 

- **Time travel.** Maybe your regular crew of _Blades in the Dark_ scoundrels are thrown 100 years into the future, fish-out-of-water. Or start with a crew of ’68 troublemakers, and they get pulled 100 years into the past at some point. Or run parallel campaigns that affect each other, one in the past and one in the future. 

- **Alternate timeline.** What would your old _Blades_ scoundrels look like if they were around in the ’60s instead? What happens if the two timelines cross over? 

## **Seasons** 

_Blades ’68_ works well when a campaign is broken into “seasons,” much like _Blades in the Dark_ . The crew going up a Tier is a good season breakpoint, but it could also be a PC going to prison, or when the crew achieves a notable victory (or suffers a notable loss). Season breaks are a great place for a time skip (page 228). 

## **Special Episodes** 

Another way to vary pacing and maintain interest is to play a “special episode” session. 

- **PC backstory.** Focus on one PC and explore a notable event from their past, or play out how one or more PCs met. Create new character sheets for the characters for this one-off session. 

- _**Blades in the Dark**_ **.** Play core _Blades_ , but deal with events in the past that are relevant to the events of your ’68 campaign. 

- **A Different Point of View.** Play a one-shot as characters from a different faction. They could be your enemies in this moment, maneuvering against the PCs. They could be your enemies in the past, showing a crucial event. Or perhaps you play out an important event in the ticking timebomb, learning how things _really_ went down. 

249 

## GM Advice 

## **Make it Groovy, Lived-in, and Weird** 

- **Make it groovy.** This is the Swinging Sixties in all its glory, from corporate modernism to radical psychedelia. Describe bold colors, pop art, sleek architecture, shag carpet, bead curtains, smoked glass, wood-paneled walls. _What here reminds us that the year is ’68?_ 

- **Make it lived-in.** The ’60s don’t exist in a vacuum; this is a city with a long and layered history. Grimy brickwork, faded signage, carpet that smells like cigarette smoke, doors that don’t close quite right. Show the wallpaper peeling, and the past bleeding through into the present. 

- **Make it weird.** The world is fantastical; there are ghosts, supernatural entities, paranormal energies, and strange cults everywhere. How does the weirdness manifest? 

Imagine a bold, modernist apartment built on top of an old brick building. Down in the basement, the foundations sink into a deep, dark pool of water. Something moves in the depths. 

That is _Blades ’68_ . 

## **GM Like a Chef, Not Like a Baker** 

Baking is a science and requires exact ratios, temperatures, and timings; deviating from a recipe can drastically impact the result. With all its numbers, procedures and mechanisms, _Blades_ can feel like baking—but actually it’s more **cooking** , like a chef. 

The recipe is still important, but there’s room for taste, feel, and instinct. A splash more wine, a pinch of salt, cooking a little longer—follow your instincts and adjust as you go. 

## **Zoom In, Zoom Out** 

Scores tend to be more **zoomed-in** ; we follow one moment to the next, with more environmental descriptions and sensory impressions. Personal business tends to be more **zoomed-out** ; we cover weeks of time, touching on various events a high level. 

Be conscious about how zoomed in you are, and try varying it. Zooming out during a score can allow for bigger and more exciting events to occur, while zooming in during personal business makes the world feel more real and alive. 

250 

## **Let the Players Drive** 

_Blades ’68_ is a sandbox game, and works best when the players drive the action—not just during a score, but through planning meetings, the claim map, and their relationships with other factions. Resist the impulse to put a hand on the steering wheel, and encourage them to go where they will. 

_“It’s like driving a car at night. You never see further than your headlights, but you can make the whole trip that way.” (E. L. Doctorow)_ 

As your campaign progresses, **personal missions** (page 236) become a useful tool for the GM to start pulling threads together, guiding events toward a narratively satisfying climax. 

Personal missions are likely to start triggering once the campaign is well underway, and each one is an opportunity that you know the crew is very likely to pursue. They’re also opportunities that you can outline yourself. Use them to push the crew into situations they’ve been avoiding, or to encourage them to advance deeper into the claim map, or whatever else you think would benefit the campaign’s progression. 

## **Help the Players Enjoy Consequences** 

The players dealing with consequences ( _Blades in the Dark_ , page 30) is a large part of what drives the game. If you want the players to embrace consequences, you need to make those consequences as fun and exciting as possible. 

- **Have a clock to throw consequences on.** Whether it’s the classic “Alert” clock or something else, make life easier by having a place to throw a clock tick or two if you can’t think of anything else. 

- **Vary consequences.** It gets stale if every consequence is the same (“Alert” clock or otherwise). Try them all out. 

- **Use reduced effect and new threats sparingly.** Introducing new threats, or giving reduced effect, can both make players feel like they’re wading through quicksand, especially on a 4/5 result. Do use them, but be intentional and mindful. A success shouldn’t feel like a punishment. 

- **Remind the players that harm isn’t so bad in ’68.** Harm only creates some minor complications after the score (page 152). Bring it on. 

- **Ask the players.** If you feel stuck, don’t be afraid to ask the players for ideas. They’ll probably go even harder than you would. 

251 

## **Let the World Catch Fire (and Explode)** 

A campaign is shorter than you think—if you play 24 sessions, that might only be 12 scores and 12 personal business phases. Let scores have real impact on the world, and make long-term project clocks an achievable size. Let stuff happen; push the accelerator, not the breaks, and just try to keep up. 

## **Pick and Choose Mechanics** 

_Blades ’68_ is designed to be highly modular, so substitute and tweak mechanics to suit you and your players. Look at chapter 9 of _Blades in the Dark_ ( **Changing the Game** , page 229), or _Blades in the Dark: Deep Cuts_ , and pick modules from there. 

_For example, you might find that experienced_ Blades _players become bulletproof in_ Blades ’68 _, due to multiple resistance rolls, more permissive harm rules, and the ability for players to reset deadlocks with a long-term project. If you feel like your campaign lacks tension and stakes, tweak the settings—you could play on “hard mode” with one resistance roll only, and deadlocks strictly permanent._ 

## **Don’t Sweat It** 

Ignore all this advice and just start playing. Try things, see how they feel, then tweak them for next time. Try, tweak, repeat. 

252 

253 

## Player Advice 

So you’ve sat down to play _Blades ’68_ and the GM has slid the book over to you, open to this page. First, thank you for playing. Second, you don’t need any advice—just play and have fun. But maybe you want some tips on how to have 

the _most_ fun; in that case, read on. 

1. The campaign will be shorter than you think. Make stuff happen now. GO IN Don’t wait, pull the trigger. HOW TO BOOTS AND ALL PLAY HARD 

2. DIG THE OTHER CHARACTERS 

Dial in on the other player characters, pay attention to their narrative, and get your own character involved. Be their fan, or be their foil. 

## 3. MAKE THE AND GM SWEAT 

Think of the game like a physics toy, and push the levers you have as hard as you can, just to see what happens. Rattle the cage, jump out the window, don’t look down. Move so fast the GM can’t keep up. 

254 

## 4. SELL Always choose the bigger reaction. Let your character be changed by the world and by the other characters. Let them THE PUNCHES be upset, emotional, irrational. HAVE FUN 5. FUCK AROUND AND FIND OUTIN ’68 

## 5. FUCK AROUND 

If your character dies, they can come back as an echo. If they have to retire, you can always make a new character. The crew cannot be stopped. Act first and think later, make bad decisions, let the chips fall where they may. 

## 6. 

## LEARN TO STOP WORRYING AND LOVE CONSEQUENCES 

Most rolls are going to have consequences. Wouldn’t it be boring to succeed all the time? Embrace consequences, and create consequences, then roll them up in a giant ball of chaos and surf on top. 

255 

## COLD OPENS 

**Cold opens** provide a way to kick off a new campaign with a short punchy score, beginning _in medias res._ The players get to introduce their characters and showcase their various abilities, while the fallout from the score will set the campaign in motion. 

Once the cold open is complete, you should move into aftermath (page 190) as normal. The crew will mark some Heat, Rep, and maybe get a payout. None of the cold opens have a guaranteed payout, but the players should have an opportunity to get some stacks—whether that’s from fencing stolen goods, selling information, or arranging a deal during the score. 

## **The Alluvium Affair Page 257** 

The crew find themselves in a stolen autopod under hot pursuit, with valuable contraband in the backseat and no client to give it to. 

## **A Thousand Feet Above Doskvol Page 260** 

The crew has a meet-and-greet with a powerful criminal faction, and everything goes wrong; now the flaming airship is plummeting toward the ground with mere moments before impact. 

## **The Frame-Up Page 264** 

A simple job collecting a package from a gobetween goes explosively wrong. The cutout is dead and the PCs are stitched up. 

## **Escaping the Mind’s Eye Page 268** 

The crew wakes up inside the resonance field. Another faction has captured them and is attempting to mine their echoes for resonance energy and information. 

256 

## The Alluvium Affair 

An anomaly wipes the first half of the score from the timeline. The crew find themselves in a stolen autopod under hot pursuit, with valuable contraband in the backseat and no client to give it to. This opening score can be used with any crew type, but is particularly well-suited to Hit Squads, Shadows, and Utopians. 

_It’s a beautiful day, with a bright blue sky above. You’re in a yellow autopod soaring through the air over Candle Bridge, Crowfoot. Behind you is a psychedelic bloom of colors. An anomaly eruption, an explosive tear in reality._ 

_Which of you is in the backseat? You’re holding a heavy canister filled with a glowing, pulsating, viscous liquid. Pure alluvium, the very substance of the resonance field; quantum instability in physical form. Rare, unstable, valuable._ 

_Which of you is driving? You have the location for the client handoff pinned to the dashboard, scrawled on a piece of paper. The handwriting disappears before your very eyes, and from your memory at the same time. Anomaly eruptions are a hell of a thing._ 

_None of you remember the first half of the score. Reality has been altered. The client no longer exists, and neither does the handoff._ 

## **Forget the Past** 

Ask each player: What other memory do you lose? 

- A cherished moment from childhood. 

- A contact from your character sheet. 

- The identity of someone close to you, like a parent or lover. 

- Something terrible you once did. 

Write it down and then cross it out. You know it, but your character doesn’t. 

## **A Glimpse of the Future** 

In the swirling slow-motion explosion of color that is the anomaly eruption, we see the PC crew in the future. Ask each player: What strikes you about this future version of you? 

- Is it a scar, a missing limb or eye? 

- Something more subtle; a visible change in demeanor? 

- Something you wear? 

- An item you carry? 

257 

_Your autopod slams back to earth and hurtles toward the end of the bridge._ _**Two autocycles** appear, one on each side, the riders pulling pistols. Up ahead is a_ _**roadblock** manned by thugs in jumpsuits. You hear the wail of sirens;_ _**three autopods** giving chase behind you._ 

Ask the two PCs in the autopod (the driver and the one in the back) what they do next. There are three goals to meet: 

- Escape with your lives 

- Find a new buyer 

- Complete the handoff 

Let the other PCs know that they can be anywhere they want—they can be in the vehicle, on a nearby rooftop, or somewhere else in the city entirely (perhaps organizing a new buyer). 

## **Factions** 

Choose the three factions involved based on the faction status and relationships of the PC crew. The more personal these factions feel, the better. 

**The Autocycles.** Pick a low- or mid-Tier faction with a paranormal flavor; Mirror House, the Tomorrow Program, or the Lounge Rats are good choices. Whoever they are, they’re currently acting ruthless, and desperate to get their hands on that alluvium. 

**The Roadblock.** Pick a higher-Tier faction (not an institution); Xantara Pharm, Strangford-Michter Financial, or New Dawn are good choices. This faction is arrogant, and feels entitled to that alluvium. 

**The Authorities.** Pick an institution; the Bluecoats, Section 6, or the Ministry of Stability are good choices. This faction is currently incensed, and determined to find a culprit for the anomaly eruption. They want to wrap this thing up quickly and go home. 

## **Potential Obstacles** 

Let the PCs drive the action, but some clock ideas are presented here. Don’t feel you have to use them—reducing or removing clocks will keep things snappier. Adjust for pacing. 

**The Authorities:** Between the roadblock and the exploding bridge, it shouldn’t be too hard to shake the authorities off your tail. 

**The Roadblock:** Concrete barricades and thugs with heavy weaponry. This probably isn’t a clock, but it is probably **limited effect** to start with; raise that to standard effect to blow right through, or risk getting bogged down in a firefight. 

258 

**The Autocycles:** This will be harder; an autocycle is one big turbine engine on two wheels, and they’ll duck and weave to keep up the chase. 

**Find a Buyer:** However they set up the meet, the PCs will have to get past guards and/or toadies to talk to the real decision-maker of the faction, and then get them to agree to a deal. If it’s happening concurrently, cut between the autopod chase and the negotiations as the clocks fill. Once the clock is filled, consider making a fortune roll to determine the agreed payout; the PC rolls an action rating of their choice (e.g. Sway or Consort), and the highest number rolled is the payout. _For example, if the highest die shows a 5, the payout is 5 stacks._ 

## **Potential Complications** 

- A resonance echo escaped from the anomaly eruption, and they materialize in the backseat—a glitched-out apparition made of multi-colored spheres of light. One of you knows them. What do they want? 

- Your autopod starts leaking plasmoleum. Start a 4-clock for how long you have before it’s empty. Will you attempt to fix it as you drive, or ditch it before it runs out of fuel? 

- You careen around the corner and find the road blocked by a large student protest. What do you do? 

- A rival is currently negotiating with the buyer when you arrive. How will you get rid of them? 

## **Locations and Flavor** 

- Concrete overpasses that soar through the air, and underpasses that plunge beneath ground. 

- Apple Lane, a narrow street lined with fashion boutiques, record stores, and music clubs. 

- Bluetime ends with a sudden snap. The sky goes dark as a cold, thick fog sweeps in. 

- Neon signs advertising cigarettes, fizzy drinks, and department stores. 

- The authorities deploy an airship, sweeping the district with a huge searchlight. 

- The canals; dank, dark, and spooky. 

## **Now What?** 

The fallout from the Alluvium Affair might play out over several sessions, or even the entire season. You could write down some key questions: 

- Who was the target; who did the PCs steal that alluvium from? 

- Who was the client; why did the PCs take that job? 

- How will that future version of the PCs come to pass, if it does? 

The answers to these questions might involve time travel or alternate dimensions or any other kind of weirdness. Let it simmer in the background. 

More immediately, the crew might want to placate the factions they antagonized during the score, or reinforce new friendships—or they might want to hit their enemies hard before they get hit back. 

259 

## A Thousand Feet Above Doskvol 

The crew has a meet-and-greet with a powerful criminal faction, and everything goes wrong. Now the flaming airship is plummeting toward the ground with mere moments before impact. This opening score can be used with any crew type, but is particularly well-suited to Racers and Dealers. 

_Doskvol. Midnight. The glittering lights of the city far below. You’ve been introduced to a powerful criminal faction and half of you are on their airship high above the city, in a huge cargo hold surrounded by piles and piles of illegal product. More than you could sell in a lifetime. There are several armed thugs, and a mid-level lieutenant is explaining their distribution pipeline to you._ 

_The other half of you are with this faction’s boss, on a tour of their opulent lair. They’re currently showing you their prize possession—a rare, huge, and very deadly pet._ 

_Who’s on the airship, and who’s talking to the boss?_ 

_Up on the airship, you’re thrown from your feet in an almighty explosion. There’s a gaping hole in the hull, black smoke billowing out. You’re gripping tightly to cargo webbing, your ears ringing, as everything in the hold flies out the hole in a vortex of rapid decompression. Product is flying past you everywhere, hurtling out into the night sky. The thugs and the lieutenant are caught by surprise and you see them tumbling away to their deaths, screaming silently._ 

If the crew are Dealers, the product on display should match whatever theirs is (at least in part). If they’re not Dealers, the product is bricks of white lotus. 

## **Factions** 

Choose two factions involved based on the faction status and relationships of the PC crew. The more personal these factions feel, the better. 

One is the high-Tier criminal faction giving the tour. SCORPION Syndicate or Strangford-Michter Financial are good options, or something like Mirror House for a more paranormal flavor. After the airship explodes, they’ll be **panicked** , **furious** , and **vengeful** . 

The other faction are the ones who planted the explosion. Again, try to pick a faction that is connected to the PCs. Red Torque, D-13, the Burn Corps, or the Palace could all be good options. 

Maybe they have their own motives, or maybe they’re being paid by someone else. Either way, they’re currently acting **professional** , **calm** , and **ruthless** . 

260 

## **Obstacles on the Airship** 

Let the PCs drive the action, but some clock ideas are presented here. Don’t feel you have to use them—reducing or removing clocks will keep things snappier. Adjust for pacing. 

Cut between action on the airship and the boss’s lair. Once the airship group gets back on the ground, the two groups might reconvene before racing back to their base—or they might not. 

**Grab That Product!** A fortune is flying past the PCs and out the hole. They’ll probably want to grab some before it’s gone. This could be a group action, with consequences as usual, but also: 

- If they’re **Dealers** , they grab a number of **units** of product equal to the **highest die roll** from the group. 

- If they’re any other crew type, note down that they have a payload of product worth **stacks** equal to the **highest die roll** from the group. They’ll have to figure out a way to move it on their own, and they might not get the full street value. 

**Airship Goes Boom:** The airship is on fire and rapidly plummeting to the ground, and will probably tear apart into tiny flaming pieces before it gets there. How will the PCs get to ground safely before that happens? Tick this clock aggressively at first to show the danger they’re in. If it fills, they’ll have no choice but to jump out immediately. _They could consider flashbacks, parachutes, passing skyscrapers..._ 

**The Saboteurs:** The faction responsible for the explosion have people on the airship who are about to make their getaway. Do the PCs bump into them? Neither group will want to hang around, but the saboteurs probably don’t want to leave any witnesses... _What’s their escape plan? Parachutes, a waiting turbofighter, or rocket packs?_ 

**Lose the Tail:** The faction who owned the airship think your group are responsible, and the Bluecoats are scrambling autopods too. You’ll have to lose the tail before you head back to base. 

## **Potential Locations & Complications** 

- A skyscraper under construction, with a towering crane. 

- Chaos, sirens, and fire engines as flaming debris rains down. 

- A squad of goons on autocycles gives chase. 

- City Council hulls have closed the street for roadworks. 

- A major canal drawbridge just starting to lift open. 

- Bluetime dawns with a swirling psychedelic sky. 

261 

## **Obstacles at the Boss’s Lair** 

The second group of PCs are being given the tour of an opulent villain’s lair, drinks in hand. They’re currently in a lounge space, being shown a very dangerous pet chained in the center. At the whispered insistence of an underling, the boss leaves the PCs momentarily, with apologies, to attend to something urgent. 

- Where is it? The wealthy districts of Whitecrown or Brightstone, or hidden beneath ground in a poorer district? Up high in a skyscraper? Out on the void sea? A luxury yacht? 

- The lair is probably secretive, secure, sophisticated, and lavish. 

- Sleek, minimalist furniture. Pod chairs and a floating fireplace. 

- Thick shag carpet. Ominous mood lighting. 

- Rotating walls and hidden panels. 

- The lair might include a security center, laboratory, interrogation room, armory, trophy room, hangar bay with submarine, art gallery, and a luxurious primary bedroom. 

A few moments later, a voice from a hidden loudspeaker tells the PCs that the airship has been sabotaged and their friends are to blame. The PCs are instructed to wait right where they are. A light flashes on the pet’s collar and the chain detaches, falling to the floor. 

**The Pet:** Is it a white tiger, a huge scorpion, or something nightmarish from the deathlands? Whatever it is, it’s huge and hungry and the PCs probably shouldn’t try to fight it. 

**Escape the Lair:** Once they’ve avoided being eaten by the pet, the PCs should probably get out of there before this high-Tier faction mobilizes all their highly skilled security teams. 

**Lose the Tail:** When the group at the base escape the property, it may be that the airship PCs land conveniently close by, and the crew regroups. Whether regrouped or not, only use one “Lose the Tail” clock as everyone tries to give their pursuers the slip. 

262 

## **Now What?** 

This cold open provides a punchy intro for the campaign and the crew, but the fallout could linger for a while. 

- Start a clock for Faction 1, as they investigate who triggered the explosion and where their missing product is. 

- Make a note of Faction 2, as they tie up loose ends (like the PCs). 

- Why did Faction 2 hit Faction 1 with the explosion? Were they paid to do it? Was it a distraction? Was there something or someone else on that airship who was the real target? 

- If you weaved in a ticking timebomb, write that down and put it on the table. 

Let these questions and clocks simmer in the background. 

More immediately, the crew might want to placate the faction they antagonized during the score, or reinforce new friendships—or they might want to hit their enemies hard before they get hit back. 

263 

## The Frame-Up 

A simple job collecting a package from a go-between goes explosively wrong. The cutout is dead and the PCs are stitched up. This opening score can be used with any crew type, but is particularly well-suited to Militants, Shadows, or crews with an interest in espionage or the political. 

_The Docks—Daggertown. Twilight. The sky is a psychedelic swirl of colors. On the corner of Biringan Street and Carronade Road stands an old 12-story brick building._ 

_A figure is thrown out the top-floor window with a muffled bang. They fall backward in a sparkling shower of glass, briefcase in one hand._ 

_Which of you is falling through the air to the alley below?_ 

_On the other side of the broken window, the rest of you stand in a large penthouse loft. It is nearly empty—some furniture covered in canvas dropcloths, a desk and typewriter, a broken window, the smoking wreckage of a metal safe. And one more thing—the dead body of your contact, killed in the explosion and lying at your feet._ 

_Your client sent you here for a simple job: pick up a package from a crime boss they couldn’t be seen associating with. Now the boss is dead, and it looks like you did it. You’ve been set up, but by who?_ 

The crew came to pick up a package from a go-between, on behalf of an espionage agent (the client). The go-between was the leader of a powerful criminal gang. This boss left their thugs downstairs and took the PCs up to the safe alone. They opened the safe and handed over the briefcase within, and then they started to apologize, said they couldn’t do it anymore, they wanted out, and before they could finish the sentence, the safe exploded. The PC accepting the briefcase was thrown out the window, and the gang leader was killed. 

_As one of you is falling 12 stories straight down, the rest of you hear three things in quick succession. First, the dead body triggers the Death Radar, and a loud klaxon sounds—Section 6 will be here soon. Second, there are sirens in the street below and the sound of the front doors being booted open—the Bluecoats are here already, someone must have tipped them off ahead of time. Third, shouts of alarm from the gang members one floor down, and the sound of scraping chairs and running feet as they come to investigate._ 

## **The Briefcase** 

What’s in the briefcase? Either decide ahead of time based on the ticking timebomb, or let the PC carrying it choose: 

264 

- Top secret intel, written in code 

- An experimental piece of technology 

- A severed human hand 

- A resonance entity held in a containment unit 

- 4 stacks of cash 

- 6 units of product 

## **Factions** 

Choose the **go-between faction** involved based on the faction status and relationships of the PC crew. The more personal, the better. Good options are the Gallo Family, the Keel Twins, the Lounge Rats, the Blackjacks, or any other mid-Tier criminal outfit. 

The dead person is one of the leaders of that faction. This leaves the other faction forces **furious** , **irrational** , and **vengeful** . Like the Bluecoats, they assume that the PCs are responsible for the murder. 

**The client** is from either Blackthorn Park or the Palace. They are **paranoid** and **ruthless** , and seem **guilty** of trying to frame the PC crew. But are they really behind it? 

**The Bluecoats** have just kicked open the front door of the building. They are acting on their orders, and under a lot of pressure to arrest the PCs. **Aggressive** , **rude** , **impatient** . 

**Section 6** will be here soon. They have significant resources and could present a major obstacle, but they’re not here for the PCs—they’re here to collect the dead body. They are **professional** and **calm** , but **threatening** . 

## **Obstacles** 

Let the PCs drive the action, but some obstacle ideas are presented here. Don’t feel you have to use them—reducing or removing clocks will keep things snappier. Adjust for pacing. 

Remember that it is **twilight** , and the psychedelic hours when bluetime is powering up or down are particularly erratic and paranormally charged. That might increase effect for paranormal actions, but also make paranormal obstacles more threatening than usual. 

First, ask the **falling PC what they do** . A fall from that height will be lethal (level 4 harm), but they have options. 

- Lines of laundry are hung between the brick buildings. 

- Below is an alley crowded with busy Daggerite food stalls, many covered with awnings and canopies, as well as fruit carts and crates of produce. 

- They might also consider a flashback. 

Once this PC gets to their feet, ask them what they want to do next. They might be able to help the other group from the outside—finding a secondary exit or fire escape, causing a distraction, etc. 

265 

**The Vengeful Gang:** The gang members will act impulsively, with no patience for a calm conversation—but once they realize the Bluecoats are in the building, they’re unlikely to stick around for long. 

**Bluecoats:** In their usual mode—demanding, quick to anger, brutal. This is a big and old building with various hallways, elevators, fire escapes, and so on. Can the crew give the authorities the slip? They won’t give up easily, so a chase through crowded streets may follow. 

**Section 6:** Their objective has nothing to do with the PCs, although they are still an institutional authority and will try to detain the wanted suspects if they cross paths. 

**The Client:** The espionage agency paying the PC crew had an agent with eyes on the handoff, and now they’re coming hard to tie up loose ends. Did the spies set up the PCs up, or are they also being played? This might be the deadly assassin **Thistle** for the Palace, or **Agent 2** for Blackthorn Park (Agent 2 is a resonance echo—remember that paranormal entities are particularly powerful during twilight). The PCs are unlikely to take either agent out, but they might be able to lose them in the streets of Daggertown. 

## **Potential Locations & Flavor** 

- The crowded streets of Daggertown, with sights, smells, and sounds from all the banyans of the Dagger Isles—the scent of Biringan starspine flowers in the air, colorful Lhan Lhaban textiles hung between buildings, a Melamati street performer doing a shadow puppet show, the rolling boom of Xis̄ran drums. 

- A festival parade blocking the street. Music, fireworks, joyful people. 

- A quiet side street. Daggerite pop music plays from an old radio. Rhythmic clacking as two old women play a game of tiles. 

- The echo of a deceased loved one is seen in the reflection of a window, warning a PC of impending danger. 

- The deeper alleys and canals of the Docks. Dark and shadowy, punctuated with sharp pools of light from overhead lamps. 

- Twilight ends with a final snap. The sky goes dark as a cold, thick fog sweeps in. 

- An agent from the opposing spy agency makes an appearance, wanting to know what’s going on. Can you enlist their aid? 

- Neon signs, tattoo parlors, lurid posters for a martial arts movie pasted on the side of an abandoned warehouse. Steam rises from a sewer grate. 

After the crew has reached safety, move into aftermath as usual. Due to the nature of the score, there is no defined payout, but the contents of the briefcase may be worth something, and so might whatever the go-between told the crew before they died. The PCs might seek out an espionage third party like the Anixis Field Unit, the Dirt Barons, or the Milliner (page 397) to sell this kind of information to. 

266 

## **Why the Setup?** 

If you’re using this cold open as the start of a campaign, look at the hooks for your chosen ticking timebomb (and the _Now What?_ questions in the next section). 

If it’s a one-shot or you’re not using a ticking timebomb, consider the following. It’s not important that these answers are all provided to the players, or that they confront the true conspirators or the conspiracy itself, but having some idea of what it could be will help make the conspiracy feel real. 

- The **go-between** was involved in a conspiracy but got cold feet. This frameup, and their death, was to tidy up a loose end. The PC crew was chosen as patsies to take the blame. The **client** was just a pawn used by the true conspirators. They had no idea the go-between was going to die, but now they want the situation cleaned up quickly and quietly. 

- **Who are the true conspirators?** They might be client’s opposite number (e.g. if the client is Blackthorn Park, the true conspirators might be the Palace). They might be a secret society, such as the Unseen, an enigma like Lord Scurlock, or another powerful faction such as the military or Section 6. 

- **What is the conspiracy?** It could be a secret project like Blueraker 5, or stealing the Bubble technology, or popping the Bubble, or releasing a Class VI Metadimensional Entity (a demon), or the development of a Null Bomb (which could create another Null Zone). The briefcase contents should relate. 

## **Now What?** 

This cold open provides a punchy intro for the campaign and the crew, but the fallout could linger for a while. You could write down some key questions: 

- What did the go-between know; what were they trying to tell the PCs? Did this knowledge lead to their death? 

- Why were the PCs framed? Are they simply convenient low-Tier scapegoats, or is there a larger reason? 

- Was the client agency directly responsible for the frame-up, or are they being played too? 

Let these questions simmer in the background. 

More immediately, the crew has been framed for a crime with major political implications that go further and deeper than they could possibly know. Until they clear their name with the major spy agencies, all their scores generate **+2 Heat** as both Blackthorn Park and the Palace keep careful tabs on the PC crew. This might take the form of unmarked autopods following them, agents on stakeout taking photos, the crew’s base being bugged, and so on. 

Clearing their name with Blackthorn Park and the Palace might very well involve working for both sides—a very dangerous game... 

267 

## Escaping the Mind’s Eye 

The crew wakes up as their echoes inside the resonance field. They don’t know this isn’t Doskvol or what’s happening to their “real” selves on the other side. Another faction has captured them and is attempting to mine their echoes for resonance energy and information. This opening score is well-suited for crews that have an affinity for the paranormal or who hold prized information others would want to extract. 

_You wake strapped to a sterile bed under bright, piercing lights. You don’t know how you got here. You’re dressed in clothes that you remember acquiring but nevertheless seem unfamiliar to you. Which of you looks into the mirrored walls of the room? Your image seems un-reflected, as though you were staring at a portrait of yourself rather than a mirror. There is medical equipment attached to your body. Your heart rate has increased since waking, and you see a device begin to flash red, and you hear a whooshing sound as a canister is propelled out of the room via pneumatic tube._ 

_Which of you was the last to fall asleep? You remember seeing people in masks standing over you, asking you questions about your past and telling you to relax. “This doesn’t have to be painful. It will all be over before you know it if you tell us what we need to know.”_ 

## **Secrets** 

Ask each player: _What secret are you most worried may be revealed?_ 

- A weakness of one of your allies. 

- An action in your past you’d rather forget. 

- A crime that implicates many. 

- Truths the world isn’t ready to know. 

Write it down and create a 6-clock with the first two segments already filled. When the clock is complete, someone, you’re not sure who yet, will learn your secret. See _Revealing Secrets_ , page 270. 

_A vague figure stands above you, unfastening the straps holding you down. “Help me get everyone else free. We’ve got strength in numbers.”_ 

_You’re in a two-story octagonal hospital theater. The walls on the lower level are mirrored and there is one set of double doors. In the shadows above, you can just make out an unoccupied observation stall._ 

_You hear the sounds of commotion, heavy boots are making haste toward you and you hear a shout. “They’ve woken early, secure the theater. Don’t let anyone out alive.”_ 

268 

## **Factions** 

Choose two factions involved based on the faction status and relationship of the PC crew. The more personal these factions feel, the better. 

**Resonance Containment.** Pick a mid-Tier faction that is capable of capturing and resonance echoes. Mirror House, Section 6, and the Palace are all good candidates. They can only hold onto the resonance echoes for so long and must work quickly. They are **rushed** and **determined** to find out the secrets. 

**Co-Captives.** Pick a low-Tier faction that is neutral or antagonistic to the crew who are trapped here as well. Anixis Field Unit, the Burn Corp, and D-13 are good candidates since they also have useful secrets. They aren’t here to help (they might even have a score to settle with the PCs), but working together might be the only way out of this. They might be resonance echoes like the PCs or doppelgangers from another reality. As far as they know, they are just “themselves.” They are **capable** , but **desperate** to get free as well. 

## **Resonance Reality** 

In order to truly escape their captors, the PCs will need to realize they are trapped in the resonance field and then find a way to escape. This is tracked on two clocks, which will advance through play. 

**Discover the Truth:** Before escaping the resonance field, the PCs must realize that they are trapped inside it. Every time they attempt to Attune, investigate the peculiar differences in their perception, interact with other resonance echoes, or give up part of their secrets, advance the clock and give them further indications this world isn’t the one they know. 

## **Things That Don’t Seem Right** 

- Mirror elements (you’re sure that person was left-handed, not right-handed). 

- Invisible walls, tessellations, or resonance disturbances. 

- Seeing dead people or doppelgangers. 

- Encountering resonance entities that don’t have a place on the other side. 

- Impossible physics. 

- Encountering the other crew who also know something is wrong. 

**Break Free:** Once the PCs know that they are trapped in the resonance field, they’ll have to break free of the bonds put on them by their captors, and return to their physical bodies. 

269 

## **Obstacles** 

Let the PCs drive the action, but here are some threats they may need to overcome. 

**The Hospital:** The PCs have no weapons. Their load is limited to one item. Orderlies with batons followed by doctors with tranquilizers will enter soon—they are all resonance projections, but the PCs don’t know that yet. Any actions taken to escape should buy them a moment of reprieve or a chance to negotiate with the other crew. This may also advance the _Discover the Truth_ clock. Once free of the hospital, the PCs find themselves on the streets of Doskvol—but they should soon realize they’re not yet truly free... 

**Trust:** Trust is a scarce commodity under these conditions. Is the other faction setting up the PCs to get caught so they can get away? Do they have a secret traitor in their midst or can they be trusted? Alternatively, the safest bet may be to do away with them altogether—strike first before they have the chance to betray you. 

**Revealing Secrets:** The faction that has trapped the PCs is trying to learn their secrets. Whenever the scoundrels would take harm or acquire Heat, or if they take a consequence that would make them vulnerable to resonance probing, advance their secret clock instead. Ask the player to describe a flashback connected to the secret and then describe one of the captors as an ominous figure appearing in the memory. 

## **Potential Complications** 

- Your reflection comes alive, stares at you confused, and runs. What do you do? 

- The world seems to tessellate and reform. When you step forward, you arrive where you stood a moment ago. You’ve reached the wall of an invisible cage. How do you get around it? 

- An involuntarily flashback to an earlier time, close to one of your secrets. Reliving it may help you find a way out of this, but it also may reveal your past to your enemies. What do you do? 

- Bluecoats are upon you with incredible speed, brandishing wicked truncheons. What do you do? 

## **Locations & Flavor** 

- Bell, a stabilized resonance entity ( _helpful, enigmatic_ ) who is trying to break into the hospital. 

- Quiet streets. The only inhabitants stare vacantly. 

- The “sky” above is dark, filled with unfamiliar stars. 

- A fight breaks out near the canal over a personal grudge. _“I loved Rorick, you just served him!”_ 

270 

- An autopod barreling toward you before it abruptly disappears. 

- A man strikes his arclighter over and over until it finally sparks to light his cigarette. He takes one pull then he strikes his arclighter over and over until it finally sparks to light his cigarette… 

## **Now What?** 

When the crew breaks free (secrets revealed or not), they likely have a bone to pick with their captors. Are they present and ready to fight, or do the PCs wake in yet another sterile abandoned place, but this time in the “real” world? Here’s some thing to consider for what happens next: 

- What did the captors learn? How will they use it to their advantage? 

- How are the PCs connected to their own resonance echoes? 

More immediately, the PCs may want to strike back at their captors. 

271 

## TICKING TIMEBOMBS 

Ticking timebombs are big issues in Doskvol 68, from street level to city-wide. A ticking timebomb is a background situation that could blow at any time. It is not the “story” of the campaign, but it will interrupt and complicate the business of the crew. Will they do anything about it? Can they? What happens when the whole thing explodes? 

## **When to Tick the Timebomb** 

Each ticking timebomb is represented by a 10-segment clock, with various events that occur as the clock ticks up. By default, tick the timebomb once per gameplay cycle (ticking it as part of the Trouble Engine makes it easy to remember). Adjust this pacing to suit your desired campaign length; for a shorter campaign, you might tick it twice per gameplay cycle, or for a longer campaign, you might tick it every second cycle. 

Like any other clock, you should also adjust it based on events in the fiction. If something happens to accelerate events, add ticks to the clock. If something happens to slow it down, you could skip adding ticks for a cycle, or even remove ticks. 

Pay attention to how engaged the players are with the ticking timebomb. If they don’t seem to care very much, don’t push it—it can just tick along in the background. Let them focus on the things that interest them. On the other hand, if they do seem focused on it, then by all means give the timebomb the spotlight. 

## **How to Stop the Timebomb** 

Not every timebomb needs to be stopped, but if the players want to, you could create a separate clock to track their efforts (perhaps a second 10-segment clock). Ticks could be added through scores, gathering information, faction plays, or separate long-term projects. 

272
